Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
The Hanafi Refutation of the Shafi'i Qiyas on Usurped Benefits
Presenting the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute and Hanafi refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on guarantee of usurped benefits and evaluate the Hanafi refutation of the Shafi'i analogy with hire.

Core Idea
Consent (rida) can impose liability for non-property, but aggression (udwan) cannot—so the analogy with hire fails.

This passage presents the full dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i. The Shafi'is argue that benefits should be guaranteed by analogy with hire contracts, where benefits are compensated. The Hanafis refute this by highlighting the role of consent: hire involves mutual agreement, which can legally make non-property compensable, whereas usurpation is aggression and does not. This distinction is a powerful example of how usul principles (like the effect of rida) shape fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Al-Shafi'i analogizes usurpation to hire: in hire, benefits are guaranteed by payment; in usurpation, even more so, so benefits should be guaranteed.
2
Identify the Hanafi response: consent vs aggression
Consent (rida) has legal effect to make non-property compensable, as seen in hire and in settlement for homicide. Aggression (udwan) lacks this effect.
This is the key distinguishing principle.
3
Analyze the additional Hanafi argument
In hire, the contract applies first to the substance (ayn) then to benefit, while usurpation has no such substitution.
Another layer showing the analogy is not exact.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi says no compensation for benefit; Shafi'i says compensation equal to hire value.

مبحث الخاص
بيان أنواع القضاء
وهو عطف على قوله : قال أبو حنيفة أي ومن أجل أن ما لا يعقل أجل أن ما لا يعقل له مثل لا يضمن شرعًا، قلنا جميعًا يعني أبا حنيفة وأبا يوسف ومحمدا جس بخلاف الشافعي له: لا يضمن منافع ما غصبه رجل بالإتلاف، وكذا بالإمساك.
وصورتها: رجل غصب فرسًا لأحد وركبه عدة مراحل، أو حبسه في بيته ولم يركب مثال للإتلاف أي منازل مثال للإمساك و لم يرسل فقال علماؤنا جميعًا: إنه لا تضمن هذه المنافع بشيء، أما بالمنافع فظاهر؛ لأنه لو ضمن بالمنافع لكان بأن يركب المالك دابة الغاصب قدر ما ركب الغاصب، أو يحبسه قدر ما حبسه الغاصب، وذلك باطل؛ للتفاوت بين راكب و راكب، وبين سير وسير، و حبس وحبس، وأما بالأعيان والمال؛ فلأن المنافع عرض لا يبقى زمانين وغير متقوم، وهو عطف الجملة على الجملة قال ابن الملك وليس "قلنا" معطوفاً على قوله قال أبو حنيفة؛ لأنه متفرع على كون الكامل سابقًا على القاصر، ولا يصلح أن يكون "قلنا" متفرعًا عليه بل هو متفرع على أن ضمان العدوان يعتمد المماثلة الكاملة أو القاصرة، وفي عبارة المصنف تسامح حيث لم يبين المتفرع عليه، والظاهر أنه معطوف على "قال" وليس كذلك. (القمر) بخلاف الشافعي إلخ قال في "التلويح" "والتحقيق": إن المنفعة ملك لا مال؛ لأن الملك ما من شأنه أن يتصرف فيه بوصف الاختصاص، والمال ما من شأنه أن يدخر للانتفاع به وقت الحاجة، والتقويم يستلزم المالية عند أبي حنيفة والملكية عند الشافعي فعنده منافع المغصوب يضمن بالغصب، وعند أبي حنيفة لا؛ لأن المنفعة عرض، والعرض غير باق وغير الباقي غير محرز وغير المحرز ليس فالمنفعة ليست بمتقومة، فلا يكون مثلاً للمال المتقوم، فلا يقضى إلا بنص ولا نص. (السنبلي) للتفاوت إلخ فإن راكبًا يعلم قوانين الركوب ولا يعلمها الآخر، والسيران يختلفان بالطريق، وتفاوت التسيير والحبسان يتفاوتان بمكان الحبس ومزاج المحبوس فليست المماثلة بين منافع الغاصب ومنافع المالك، وقيل: إنه لا يمكن الحكم بالمماثلة في الأعراض؛ لأن العرض كلما وجد اضمحل، فلا يتحقق المماثلة. (القمر) فلأن المنافع إلخ تقريره أن المنافع عرض، وكل عرض لا يبقى زمانين فالمنافع لا تبقى زمانين، وغير الباقي غير محرز، فالمنافع غير محرزة، وكل غير محرز غير متقوم فالمنافع غير متقومة، بخلاف المال، فإنه جوهر باق متقوم فلا تماثل بين المال والمنافع أما صغرى الأول (أي القياس الأول فظاهر وأما كبرى الأول، فلأن البقاء عرض، فلو كان للعرض بقاء لزم قيام العرض بالعرض وهو باطل؛ فإن القيام هو التبعية في التحيز ولا تحيز للعرض بخلاف المال فلا تماثل بينهما، وإنما ضمناها بالمال في الإجارة؛ لأن للرضا تأثيرًا في إيجاب الأصول والفضول جميعًا، ولا تأثير للعدوان فيه، والشافعي ليه يقول: بضمانها بالمال منافع الغصب بقدر العرف في كرائها إلى ذلك المنزل قياسًا على الإجارة والوجه ما قلنا، ولابد لك الدابة حينئذ من الفرق بين المنافع والزوائد، فالمنافع كركوب الدابة والحمل عليها، والزوائد كالنسل للدابة واللبن لها والثمرة للشجرة ،ونحوها فالمغصوب بنفسه يضمن بالهلاك والاستهلاك جميعًا، والزوائد تضمن بالاستهلاك دون الهلاك، والمنافع لا تضمن بالاستهلاك أي الولد والهلاك، فعبر المصنف عن الاستهلاك بالإتلاف و لم يذكر الهلاك، وهو الحبس، = وفيه كلام في الكلام وأما كبرى الثاني؛ فلأن الاحتراز عبارة عن الصيانة والادخار لوقت الحاجة، وهذا يتوقف على البقاء وأما كبرى الثالث؛ فلأن شرط التقوم الإحراز ، ألا ترى أن الحشيش في المفازة ليس له إحراز فليس هو متقوم، وللشافعي أن يمنع هذه الكبرى ويقول: لا نسلم أن شرط التقوم الإحراز بل التقوم باعتبار الملكية وإطلاق التصرف كذا في "التلويح". (القمر) وإنما ضمناها إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقريره أن المنافع وإن كانت أعراضاً غير باقية فلها حكم الأعيان الباقية في الشرع حتى يرد عليها عقدة الإجارة، فالمنافع تضمن بالإجارة فمن استأجر دابة ليركبها مرحلتين بدرهمين مثلاً، فأخذ منافع الدابة أعطى عرضها، فكذا تضمن منافع الغصب أيضًا. (القمر) تأثيرًا إلى ألا ترى أن بالرضاء يجب المال في مقابلة ما ليس بمال كما في الصلح عن دم العمد على المال، ويجب الفضل والمنافع أيضًا كما في بيع عبد قيمته ألف بألوف، فيجب أصل المال والفضل بالتراضي، ولا يثبت شيء من ذلك بالعدوان بحال، فالمنافع في الإجارة تضمن، لتحقق الرضاء لا في الغصب؛ لأن الغصب عدوان لا يقال إن المال في قتل الخطاء يجب بمقابلة ما ليس بمال بالعدوان؛ لأنا نقول: إن وجوب المال هناك ليس بمحض العدوان بل بخطر المحل؛ لئلا تهدر النفس المحترمة مجانًا. (القمر) الأصول: أي الأعيان كما في المستأجر. (المحشي) والفضول: أي ما ليس بأعيان وأموال كما على المؤجر. (المحشي) للعدوان فيه : أي في إيجاب الأصول والفضول . (القمر) والوجه أي وجه الفرق في الإجارة والغصب. (القمر) ما قلنا إلخ أي من أن للرضاء تأثيرًا في إيجاب الأصول والفضول، ولا رضاء في الغصب بل فيه عدوان، فلا تأثير فيه أيضًا، وأيضًا فرق أخر بين الإجارة والغصب وهو أن ورود العقد على المنافع في الإجارة بإقامة العين مقام المنفعة للحاجة، ولهذا لو قال أجرت منافع هذا الدار شهرًا هكذا لم يجز، فعلم أن العقد يرد على العين أولاً، ثم ينتقل إلى المنفعة، فالقياس مع الفارق . (السنبلي) وهو الحبس أي ملاك المنافع الحبس. (القمر)
Section on al-Khass (the specific). Explanation of types of rulings. It is connected to his statement: 'Abu Hanifa said' meaning: because what is not known to have a like is not guaranteed legally. We all said meaning Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, unlike al-Shafi'i who says: the benefits of what a man usurps are not guaranteed by destruction, and likewise by detention. Its example: A man usurped someone's horse and rode it several stages, or detained it in his house without riding (example of destruction) (or detention) and did not set it free. So our scholars all said: these benefits are not guaranteed by anything. As for [guarantee] by benefits, it is apparent; because if they were guaranteed by benefits, it would mean that the owner rides the usurper's beast to the extent the usurper rode, or detains it to the extent he detained, and that is invalid due to variation between rider and rider, travel and travel, detention and detention. As for [guarantee] by specific objects and property, it is because benefits are an accident that does not persist for two moments and are non-valuable. It is an apposition of sentence to sentence. Ibn al-Malik said: 'We said' is not conjoined to 'Abu Hanifa said' because it is a branch of the perfect preceding the deficient, but it is a branch of the principle that guarantee of aggression depends on complete or imperfect similarity. In the author's expression there is tolerance as he did not specify the branched-upon thing. Apparently it is conjoined to 'said' and it is not. (al-Qamar) Unlike al-Shafi'i etc. It is said in al-Talwih and al-Tahqiq: Benefit is ownership not property; because ownership is that which can be disposed of by description of special right, and property is that which can be stored for use at time of need. Evaluation necessitates property in Abu Hanifa's view and ownership in al-Shafi'i's view. So in his view, benefits of usurped property are guaranteed by usurpation, but in Abu Hanifa's view no; because benefit is an accident, and an accident is non-permanent, and non-permanent is non-secured, and non-secured is not valuable. So benefit is non-valuable, so it cannot be a like for valuable property, and it cannot be ruled except by text, and there is no text. (al-Sunbuli) Due to variation etc. For a rider may know the rules of riding and another may not. Travels differ by road. Variation in riding and detentions differ by place of detention and temperament of the detained. So there is no similarity between the usurper's benefits and the owner's benefits. It is said that similitude cannot be judged in accidents because whenever an accident exists it perishes, so similitude is not realized. (al-Qamar) So because benefits etc. Its exposition: Benefits are accidents, and every accident does not persist for two moments, so benefits do not persist for two moments. Non-permanent is non-secured, so benefits are non-secured. Everything non-secured is non-valuable, so benefits are non-valuable, unlike property, for it is a permanent substance, valuable, so no similarity between property and benefits. As for the minor premise (i.e. of the first syllogism), it is apparent. As for the major premise of the first, because permanence is an accident; if an accident had permanence, it would necessitate the subsistence of accident by accident, which is invalid; for subsistence is dependency in spatial occupation, and an accident has no spatial occupation, unlike property so no similarity between them. However we guarantee them by property in hire because consent has effect in imposing both original [substances] and superfluities, and aggression has no effect in it. Al-Shafi'i says: they are guaranteed by property (benefits of usurpation) to the extent of custom in its hire to that place, by analogy with hire. The correct view is what we said. You must distinguish between benefits and accretions: benefits like riding the beast and loading on it, accretions like offspring of the beast, its milk, fruit of the tree and the like. So the usurped item itself is guaranteed by destruction and consumption both; accretions are guaranteed by consumption not destruction; benefits are not guaranteed by consumption or destruction. So the author expressed consumption as destruction and did not mention destruction, which is detention. And there is discussion in the discussion. As for the major premise of the second: because preservation is safeguarding and storing for time of need, and this depends on permanence. As for the major premise of the third: because the condition of valuation is security; do you not see that grass in the desert has no security so it is not valuable. Al-Shafi'i may deny this major and say: we do not accept that the condition of valuation is security; rather valuation is by virtue of ownership and unrestricted disposal, as in al-Talwih. (al-Qamar) 'We guarantee them etc.' is a rebuttal of an estimated objection: benefits although they are non-permanent accidents, they have the ruling of permanent substances in sharia such that the contract of hire applies to them. So benefits are guaranteed by hire. For if someone hires a beast to ride two stages for two dirhams, he takes the benefits of the beast and gives its equivalent. So likewise benefits of usurpation would be guaranteed. (al-Qamar) 'Effect' means: do you not see that by consent property is due in exchange for what is not property, as in settlement for intentional killing for property, and surplus and benefits also become due as in selling a slave worth a thousand for thousands. So the original property and surplus are due by mutual consent, and nothing of that is established by aggression at all. So benefits in hire are guaranteed due to presence of consent, not in usurpation because usurpation is aggression. It cannot be said that property in accidental killing is due in exchange for what is not property by aggression; because we say: the obligation of property there is not purely due to aggression but due to the gravity of the locus, so that a respected life is not wasted for free. (al-Qamar) 'al-Usul' means indivisible substances as in the hirer. (al-Muhashi) 'al-Fudul' means what is not substances and property as on the lessor. (al-Muhashi) 'Due to aggression in it' means in imposing original substances and superfluities. (al-Qamar) 'The correct view' meaning the aspect of difference between hire and usurpation. (al-Qamar) 'What we said etc.' means that consent has effect in imposing original substances and superfluities, and there is no consent in usurpation but aggression, so it has no effect there. Also there is another difference between hire and usurpation: the contract applies to benefits in hire by setting the substance in place of benefit due to need, and so if he said 'I hired the benefits of this house for a month like this', it is not valid. So it is known that the contract applies to the substance first then transfers to benefit. So the analogy is dissimilar. (al-Sunbuli) 'It is detention' meaning the destruction of benefits is detention. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (usul dispute) between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the guarantee of benefits (manafi') in cases of usurpation (ghasb). The Hanafis hold that benefits are not guaranteed at all, whether by destruction (e.g., riding) or by detention (e.g., confining the animal). Imam al-Shafi'i holds that benefits are guaranteed by assessing their market value through analogy with hire (ijarah). The text explains the Hanafi reasoning in detail. First, if benefits were to be guaranteed in kind (by similar benefits), it would be impossible due to variation in riding ability, duration, etc. Second, benefits are classified as 'accidents' (a'rad) that do not persist over time and are not considered 'property' (mal) in the Hanafi definition because they cannot be stored (iddikhar). Therefore they are non-valuable (ghayr mutaqawwim) and cannot be a 'like' (mithl) for property. The passage also distinguishes between benefits (manafi') and accretions (zawa'id). Accretions like offspring or fruit are guaranteed by consumption (istihlak) but not by destruction (halak). Benefits are not guaranteed by either. The Hanafi position is that consent (rida) in contracts like hire can impose obligations on benefits because consent has legal effect, but aggression (udwan) does not have such effect. The passage also addresses the Shafi'i analogy with hire: hire involves consent, while usurpation involves aggression, so the analogy fails. This is a classic example of Hanafi usul applying definitions of mal, taqwim, and ihraz to derive a fiqh ruling.

Key Terms
الخاص (al-khass): The specific (opposite of general); the passage is from a section on al-Khass in usul al-fiqh.
المنفعة (al-manfi'ah): Benefit or utility derived from a property, e.g., riding an animal, living in a house.
التقوم (al-taqwim): Valuability; being recognized as property with market value.
الإحراز (al-ihraz): Securing or possessing in a way that allows storage and preservation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, why does the Shafi'i analogy with hire fail?
A) Because usurpation is more severe than hire
B) Because hire involves consent while usurpation involves aggression
C) Because benefits in hire are different
D) Because there is no text for guarantee

Key Takeaway
Consent (rida) can make non-property compensable, but aggression (udwan) cannot; this principle refutes the Shafi'i qiyas on usurped benefits.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

Types of Hasan li 'Aynihi: Three Categories
Defines and categorizes hasan li 'aynihi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hasan li 'aynihi, distinguish its three subtypes, and apply the concept by correctly classifying examples and explaining the role of suqut (falling) and tarakhkhus (concession).

Core Idea
Hasan li 'aynihi: intrinsic goodness that is perpetual, conditional, or resembles another, but all are good in essence.

This passage from Nur al-Anwar defines the foundational usul category of hasan li 'aynihi (good for its own essence) and breaks it into three types. Understanding this classification is crucial for distinguishing acts that are always good from those whose obligation may drop under excused circumstances, and for correctly labeling acts that appear to have external cause but are intrinsically good. The commentary clarifies the subtle point that 'falling' applies to obligation, not to the inherent goodness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined and its opposite
Locate the definition of hasan li 'aynihi and note its opposite, hasan li ghayrihi. The text says the goodness is 'in the very thing for which it is established without intermediary'.
The opposite is hasan li ghayrihi (good due to another).
2
Understand the three types of hasan li 'aynihi
The matn lists three types: (1) does not accept falling (suqut) at all, (2) accepts falling at some time due to an excuse, and (3) attached to the first but resembles good for another. Focus on the key phrase: 'either does not accept falling or accepts it' and the third type's dual nature.
The third type is also called hasan li 'aynihi but has a semblance of being hasan li ghayrihi.
3
Distinguish between suqut of the goodness and suqut of the obligation
The commentary emphasizes that when the text says 'does not accept falling', it refers to the goodness (husn) not the act itself. The falling (suqut) is of the obligation (wujub). For example, in coercion, the goodness of confession remains; the obligation may drop as a concession.
This distinction prevents the misunderstanding that the act becomes bad when its obligation falls.
4
Analyze the third type: why it belongs to hasan li 'aynihi despite an intermediary
The passage explains that the third type has an intermediary in form but not in meaning. The underlying meaning (the intrinsic quality) outweighs the form, so it is classified as hasan li 'aynihi. This is a key usul subtlety about prioritizing meaning over appearance.
The intermediary (wasta) here is something external, but the goodness is ultimately in the act's essence.

Source Text

مبحث الخاص ١٢٦ بيان أنواع الحسن فقال: وهو إما أن يكون لعينه أي الحسن إما أن يكون لذات المأمور به، بأن يكون الحسن حسنه في ذات ما وضع له ذلك من غير واسطة وهذا ثلاثة أنواع على ما قال: أي المأمور به وهو إما أن لا يقبل السقوط أو يقبله أي لا يقبل ذلك الحسن السقوط من المأمور به بل يكون دائما حسنًا ومأمورًاً به على المكلف وواجبا عليه أو يقبل السقوط في حين من الأحيان لعذر من الأعذار. المأمور به أو يكون ملحقًا بهذا القسم لكنه مشابه لما حسن لمعنى في غيره أي يكون المأمور به ملحقا بالحسن لعينه لكنه مشابه للحسن لغيره، فهو ذو جهتين، وإنما جعله من أقسام الحسن حسنه في ذات ما وضع إلخ يقال له الحسن لعينه ولمعنى في نفسه، وخلاصة تعريفه أنه ما اتصف بالحسن الحسن ثبت في ذاته ومقابله ما اتصف بالحسن الحسن ثبت في غيره يقال له الحسن لغيره والحسن لمعنى في غيره. (السنبلي) من غير واسطة أي بلا واسطة في العروض بأن يكون صفة الحسن ثابتة لتلك الواسطة، وتنسب إلى هذا المأمور به مجازا في حسنه وإن كان للغير فيه دخل ما القمر لا يقبل السقوط أصلاً ووصفا، أو وصفًا فقط. (إفاضة الأنوار (٤٦) أي لا يقبل إلخ إيماء إلى أن ضمير هو يرجع إلى الحسن الأولى أن يرجع إلى المأمور به الحسن لعينه ليلائم ما ذكره المصنف في المثال بقوله كالتصديق إلخ فإن هذه أمثلة المأمور به على أن الحسن لا يقبل السقوط، فإن الساقط في حال الإكراه هو وجوب الإقرار لا حسن الإقرار حتى لو صبر عليه وقتل كان مأجورا، اللهم إلا أن يقال: إن حسن الإقرار أيضًا ساقط إلا أن سقوطه باعتبار الترخص كسقوط الصوم للمسافر، فمن أقر ولم يقبل الرخصة أتى فرضًا، فيكون مأجورًا كذا في بعض شروح أصول فخر الإسلام. (القمر) لا يقبل ذلك الحسن إلخ بإظهار فاعل لا يقبل أشار إلى دفع الوهم وهو أن الظاهر من عبارة الماتن أن الحسن لعينه الذي هو قسم من المأمور به يقبل السقوط، ولا يتصور معنى سقوطه ههنا؛ لأن المأمور به لا يسقط بل الساقط التكليف به، والتكليف غير المأمور به ودفعه: بأن الضمير في لا يقبل راجع إلى الحسن الذي ذكر في ضمن الحسن أي المأمور به والمراد بعدم سقوط حسنه عدم سقوط التكليف به، وكذا ضمير يقبل أيضاً راجع إليه فافهم. (السنبلي) وإنما جعله إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقريره أن هذا القسم ذو جهتين، فلم لم يجعل من أقسام الحسن بمعنى في غيره، وحاصل الدفع أنه إنما جعل من أقسام الحسن لعينه اعتبارًا للأصل أي المعنى، فإن المعنى راجح على الصورة؛ هو المقصود دون الصورة، ففي هذا القسم وإن وجدت الواسطة صورة لكنها منعدمة معنى على ما ستقف عليه فباعتبار المعنى جعل من أقسام الحسن لعينه. (القمر)
Section on the Specific [PAGE_127] Statement of the types of good. He said: It (good) is either for its own essence, i.e., good is for the essence of the commanded act, such that the goodness is in the very thing for which it is established without intermediary. And this is three types, as he said: (i.e., the commanded act) either does not accept falling (suqut) or accepts it. That is, that goodness does not accept falling from the commanded act, but rather is always good and commanded upon the responsible person and obligatory upon him, or it accepts falling at some time due to an excuse. The commanded act or is attached to this category but resembles what is good due to a meaning in another, i.e., the commanded act is attached to the good for its own essence but resembles the good for another, so it has two aspects. He only made it a part of the divisions of good because its goodness is in the very thing for which it is established, etc. It is called good for its own essence and for a meaning in itself. Summary of its definition: It is that which is described by goodness, goodness established in its essence. Its opposite is that which is described by goodness, goodness established in another, called good for another and good for a meaning in another. (al-Sunbuli) Without intermediary means without an intermediary in occurrence, such that the attribute of goodness is established for that intermediary, and is attributed to this commanded act metaphorically in its goodness, even if there is some involvement of another. (al-Qamar) It does not accept falling essentially and descriptively, or descriptively only. (Ifadat al-Anwar (46)) Meaning 'does not accept' etc. is an indication that the pronoun 'huwa' refers to the good. It is better that it refers to the commanded act that is good for its own essence to match what the author mentioned in the example by saying 'like affirmation' etc., for these are examples of the commanded act such that the goodness does not accept falling. For what falls in the case of coercion is the obligation of confession, not the goodness of confession, even if he patiently endured and was killed, he would be rewarded. Unless it is said that the goodness of confession also falls, but its falling is by way of concession, like the falling of fasting for the traveler. If one confessed and did not accept the concession, he performed an obligatory act, so he is rewarded. Thus in some commentaries on Usul of Fakhr al-Islam. (al-Qamar) 'That goodness does not accept' etc. By showing the agent of 'does not accept', he indicated repelling the misunderstanding that apparently from the wording of the matn, the good for its own essence, which is a part of the commanded act, accepts falling. But here it is inconceivable to fall because the commanded act does not fall; rather the falling is the obligation of it, and the obligation is other than the commanded act. He repelled it by saying that the pronoun in 'does not accept' refers to the good mentioned within the good, i.e., the commanded act, and what is meant by the non-falling of its goodness is the non-falling of the obligation of it. Similarly, the pronoun of 'accepts' also refers to it, so understand. (al-Sunbuli) 'He only made it' etc.: Repelling an anticipated objection. Its summary is that this category has two aspects, so why was it not made part of the divisions of good in the sense of 'for another'? The gist of the repelling is that it was made part of the divisions of good for its own essence by considering the origin (i.e., the meaning), for meaning outweighs form; it is the intended, not form. So in this category, even if there is an intermediary in form, it is absent in meaning, as you will see. So by considering meaning, it was made part of the divisions of good for its own essence. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage defines and divides 'hasan li 'aynihi' (good for its own essence) as opposed to 'hasan li ghayrihi' (good for another). The matn states that goodness is either intrinsic to the commanded act itself without any intermediary, and this has three types. The first type is that which never ceases to be good—its goodness is perpetual and the obligation remains (e.g., belief in God). The second type is that which can drop at times due to an excuse (e.g., reciting the basmalah in prayer—its goodness remains but the ruling may be relaxed). The third type is that which is attached to the first category but resembles goodness for another; it has two aspects but is ultimately considered good for its own essence because the underlying meaning (the intrinsic quality) outweighs the form (the external resemblance). The commentary clarifies a potential confusion: when the text says 'does not accept falling' or 'accepts falling', it refers to the goodness of the act, not the act itself. So in the example of confession under coercion, the goodness of confession does not fall; if one withstands coercion and confesses, he is rewarded. The falling is of the obligation, not the goodness. The comments also address why the third type is classified under hasan li 'aynihi despite having an intermediary: because the intermediary is only in form, not in meaning; the meaning of goodness is directly in the act itself.

Key Terms
الحسن لعينه (al-hasan li 'aynihi): Goodness that is intrinsic to the act itself, not derived from something else.
الحسن لغيره (al-hasan li ghayrihi): Goodness that is derived from another act or consideration, not intrinsic.
السقوط (al-suqut): Falling or removal of the obligation or goodness; here specifically the removal of the obligation while the intrinsic goodness remains possible.
الترخص (al-tarakhkhus): Concession; allowance that permits dropping the obligation while the act remains good.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the first type of hasan li 'aynihi (that does not accept falling)?
A) Reciting basmalah in prayer
B) Belief (iman) in God
C) Fasting on the day of 'Ashura
D) Giving charity when one is in debt

Key Takeaway
Hasan li 'aynihi encompasses acts whose goodness is intrinsic; they may be perpetual, conditionally obligatory, or resemble external cause, but are all classified as intrinsically good by prioritizing meaning over form.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
The Absolute Command: Immediacy or Delay?
Determining whether an absolute command requires immediate or delayed action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether an absolute command implies immediacy or delay, explain the rational proof (qiyas jaliy) used, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, identifying the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
An absolute command implies delay, not immediacy — requiring fawr would contradict its nature of ease.

This passage addresses a fundamental usul question: when Allah or the Prophet issues a command without a time restriction, must it be performed at once, or may it be delayed? The Hanafi school, relying on the inherent nature of such commands as facilitative, holds that delay is permitted without sin until death nears. This lesson unpacks the proof and the disagreement with al-Karkhi and the Shafi'is, providing a clear understanding of the Hanafi ruling on absolute commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of command under discussion
The author distinguishes between absolute (muṭlaq) and time-bound (muqayyad) commands. Focus on absolute commands, which are not tied to a time whose passing would invalidate the act (e.g., zakat, sadaqat al-fitr).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
According to the Hanafi school, an absolute command implies delay (tarākhī). One does not sin by delaying until the end of life or when death is imminent.
Delay does not mean preference for postponement, but simply that no sin for delaying.
3
Examine the opposing view
Al-Karkhi and Shafi'is hold that such commands imply immediacy (fawr). They cite the example of a master commanding a slave to fetch water, where immediate action is expected.
This is based on custom, not the bare command.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The proof is qiyas jaliy: the subject (mawḍūʿ) of the absolute command is taysīr (ease). If it implied immediacy, it would contradict its own nature. Also, without contextual indication (qarīna), custom alone does not impose immediacy.
The proof is decisive (qat'ī) in meaning, even though the transmission is zannī.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Hanafi: one may delay payment of zakat until near death without sin. Shafi'i: one sins if he delays without valid excuse.
This difference impacts fatwa and practice.

وهو على التراخي خلافًا للكرخي أي هذا الأمر المطلق محمول عندنا على التراخي بل يسع ه، وعند الكرخي طلبه لابد فيه من الفور
يعني لا يجب الفور في أدائه بل تأخيره، احتياطًا لأمر العبادة بمعنى أنه يأثم بالتأخير لا بمعنى أنه يصير قاضيًا، وعندنا لا يأثم إلا في آخر العمر ، أو حين إدراك علامات الموت ولم يؤد فيه. ودليلنا هو ما أشار إليه بقوله : لئلا يعود على موضوعه بالنقض يعني موضوع الأمر المطلق كان هو التيسير والتسهيل ، فلو كان محمولاً على الفور لعاد على موضوعه بالنقض، ويكون مناقضا للموضوع.

(القمر)
لا يجب الفور إلخ وعلى هذا المعنى فلا يرد قول المصنف وهو على التراخي إلخ، أنه يفهم عنه أن التراخي في الأمر أحب وهو كما ترى فاسد ؛ لأن الشارح بهذا الكلام بين المراد بقول المصنف: هو على التراخي نفي وجوب الفور لا وجوب التأخير بقرينة تقابل قول الكرخي فافهم. Chapter on Specific Rulings. Explanation of the implication of the command regarding the ruling of time. For it indicates the obligation of presenting expiation before breaking the oath, and the obligation of presenting it was abrogated by consensus, but its permissibility remained according to him (al-Shafi'i) and did not remain according to us at all. Explanation of the implication of the command regarding the ruling of time. Then when the author finished discussing the goodness of the commanded thing and its appendages, he began to explain its division into absolute and time-bound, and said: The command is of two types: absolute with respect to time, i.e., one of them is an absolute command not restricted to a time whose passing would cause it to be lost, like zakat and sadaqat al-fitr, for after the existence of the cause, i.e., ownership of wealth and head, and the condition, i.e., the passing of the year and the day of Fitr, they are not restricted to a time that would be lost with its passing, but whenever it is performed it is an adā’ not a qadā’, though it is recommended to hasten. Expiation. Oath. For it indicates etc.: This is if in the narration of the hadith there is the word “thumma” as in the narration transmitted by Abu Dawud in his Sunan, and from Abd al-Rahman ibn Samura said: The Messenger of Allah said: If you swear an oath and see something better, then expiate your oath and do what is better, agreed upon. And al-Tirmidhi and Muslim narrated from Abu Hurayra that the Messenger of Allah said: Whoever swears an oath and sees something better, let him expiate his oath and do it. These narrations do not indicate the obligation of presenting expiation before breaking the oath, but rather indicate combining the breaking and the expiation without addressing precedence or delay. (al-Qamar) And it did not remain according to us at all, but if he presents the expiation, nothing is taken back from the poor according to us, because it occurred as a voluntary charity. Then know that the disagreement between us and al-Shafi'i is only concerning financial expiation, for expiation by fasting is not permissible before breaking the oath by consensus, as in Mishkat al-Anwar. (al-Qamar) Its division, i.e., division of the commanded thing. And then the intended meaning of “command” in the author’s statement “The command is of two types” is the commanded thing, and this is a linguistic metaphor, supported by the author’s example of zakat and sadaqat al-fitr, for they are commanded. (al-Qamar) Would be lost with its passing: i.e., that commanded thing’s performance would be lost with the passing of that time. It was restricted by this to distinguish between absolute and time-bound; otherwise, the absolute is also time-bound in the sense that it occurs in time. (al-Qamar) “Would be lost with its passing” etc. This is a rebuttal of an objection: that the existence of an absolute command without time is impossible, for every command occurs in time and is not free from time in any state. So he answered that the intended meaning of absolute is a command not restricted to a time whose passing would cause it to be lost, not that it is free from time, so understand. (al-Sunbuli) Ownership of wealth: i.e., the nisab, and this is the cause for the obligation of zakat. (al-Qamar) And the head, and this is the cause for the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr. (al-Qamar) Explanation of the implication of the command regarding the ruling of time. And it (the absolute command) implies delay, contrary to al-Karkhi, i.e., this absolute command is considered by us to imply delay, rather it allows it. According to al-Karkhi, its demand necessarily requires immediacy. He means that immediacy is not obligatory in its performance, rather delay is permitted as a precaution for the matter of worship, in the sense that one would sin by delaying, not in the sense that he becomes a repeater (qāḍin). According to us, one does not sin except at the end of life, or when signs of death appear and he has not performed it. Our proof is what he indicated by his statement: Lest it return to its subject with contradiction, meaning the subject of the absolute command is ease and facilitation. If it were considered to imply immediacy, it would return to its subject with contradiction and would be contradictory to the subject. For al-Karkhi, i.e., Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi from us, and for the Shafi'is. Al-Karkhi is a locality in Baghdad and a village of Baghdad, as was said. In al-Kashf, al-Karkhi narrated from our companions that it is on immediacy, and it is the saying of the majority of hadith scholars and some Mu'tazila. Abu Sahl al-Zajjaji mentioned that according to Abu Yusuf it is on immediacy, and according to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i it is on delay. And it was narrated from Abu Hanifa like the saying of Abu Yusuf. (al-Qamar) Immediacy is not obligatory, etc., i.e., it is not obligatory to perform it at the earliest possible time. The intended meaning of delay is not restriction to the future, such that if one performs immediately he is not discharged. Immediacy originally is a verbal noun: one says fārat al-qidr when it boils, then it was metaphorically used for speed. (al-Qamar) Immediacy is not obligatory, etc. On this meaning, the author's statement “It implies delay” etc. is not subject to the objection that it suggests delay is preferable, which is evidently unsound; because the commentator by this statement explained the intended meaning of the author's statement “it implies delay” as negation of obligation of immediacy, not obligation of delay, by the clue of its contrast with al-Karkhi's statement, so understand. (al-Sunbuli) Necessarily requires, etc. And they argued for this by saying that if a master says to his slave “Give me water to drink,” it is understood that he must hurry in giving water, so that the slave is blamed in the view of rational people if he delays. Thus the command is considered to imply immediacy. The response: The discussion is about the command that is free from contextual indications, and the certainty of immediacy in the mentioned example is due to custom and habit, and there is no argument in it. (al-Qamar) He sins by delaying, etc., because delay is a loss since one does not know whether he will be able to perform in the second time or not, and loss is forbidden. The response: We do not concede that delay is a loss because he is able to perform in any part of the time he reaches. As for sudden death, it is rare and cannot be the basis for legal rulings. (al-Qamar) Lest it return: i.e., lest it go back to its subject, i.e., its meaning or benefit, clothed with contradiction, i.e., contradicting its subject, which is absoluteness. (al-Qamar) Would return, etc.: i.e., its subject would become invalid and null. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and proof from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary explaining the implication of an absolute command with respect to time. The author first discusses the issue of expiation (kaffārah) before breaking an oath, noting that the obligation to pre-empt expiation was abrogated by consensus, but al-Shafi'i still permits it while the Hanafi school does not. This serves as an example of how commands relate to time. Then the main topic: the absolute command (amr muṭlaq) is not restricted to a specific time whose passing would nullify the act; examples are zakat and sadaqat al-fitr, which are obligatory but can be performed at any time after the cause and condition are met, though it is recommended to do so early. The core ruling is that the absolute command implies delay (tarākhī), meaning that one is not required to perform the act immediately; rather, one may delay it without sin until the end of life or when death is imminent. This is the Hanafi position, contrary to al-Karkhi and the Shafi'is who hold that the command implies immediacy (fawr). The proof is rational: the subject of an absolute command is ease and facilitation; if it required immediacy, that would contradict its nature. The commentator elaborates that 'delay' here means it is not obligatory to act at once, not that it is preferable to delay. The Hanafi position is also supported by the absence of any contextual indication requiring speed. The passage also clarifies that according to Abu Yusuf (and a narration from Abu Hanifa) the command implies immediacy, while according to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i it implies delay. The relied-upon position is delay.

Key Terms
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-muṭlaq): A command not restricted to a specific time whose passing would cause the act to be lost.
الفور (al-fawr): Immediacy; requiring performance at the first possible moment without delay.
التراخي (al-tarākhī): Delay; allowing performance at any time within one's lifespan without sin until near death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling of an absolute command regarding time?
A) It requires immediate action (fawr).
B) It implies delay (tarākhī), and one does not sin unless death approaches without performing.
C) It must be performed exactly at a specific time.
D) It is optional to perform or not.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that an absolute command implies delay (tarākhī), not immediacy, based on the nature of ease inherent in such commands.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Triple Role of Time: Container, Condition, and Cause in Hanafi Usul
Defining four types of time as container, condition, or cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between time as a container, condition, and cause for a commanded act, and apply the Hanafi rule that preposing an act is impermissible when time is both a condition for permissibility and a cause for obligation.

Core Idea
Time for prayer is container, condition, and cause — preposing is blocked because conditionality for validity and causality combine.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar unpacks the complex relationship between time and commanded acts in Hanafi usul. The author defines three distinct functions time can serve: container (zarf), condition (shart), and cause (sabab). Using the example of prayer times, the commentary then addresses a subtle objection about preposing the act before its time, distinguishing between conditions for obligation and conditions for validity. This lesson walks you through the definitions, the example, and the crucial ruling on preposing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three functions of time
From the passage, extract the definition of container, condition, and cause. Note the distinction between ظرف (container) and معيار (measure).
The container (zarf) means time exceeds the act; condition means act invalid before time; cause means time has an effect on obligation.
2
Analyze the example of prayer time
See how prayer time fits all three roles simultaneously. Understand why it is valid only in its time and why the time exceeds the act.
Prayer time is a container because it can accommodate more acts; a condition because prayer before time is invalid; a cause because obligation is attributed to it.
3
Examine the objection about preposing
The objection asks: if time is a condition, why can't we pray before the time, just as zakat can be given before the year? Identify the Hanafi response.
The response hinges on the type of condition: zakat's year is a condition for obligation; prayer's time is a condition for permissibility.
4
Distinguish between شرط الوجوب and شرط الجواز
Understand that time for prayer is a condition for validity (شرط الجواز) not merely for obligation. Preposing is only allowed when the condition is for obligation.
A condition for obligation allows preposing because the obligation is not yet triggered; a condition for permissibility does not, as the act is only valid within the condition.
5
Apply the combined rule
Since time is both a condition for permissibility and a cause for obligation, preposing is absolutely impermissible. Confirm with the text.
The passage states: 'Here, since conditionality and causality are combined, inevitably preposing upon the time is not permissible.'

بيان موجب الأمر في حكم الوقت
فإن الوقت فيها يفضل عن الأداء إذا أدى على حسب السنة من غير إفراط، فيكون ظرفًا، الوقت ولا يصح الأداء قبل دخول الوقت ويفوت بفوته، فيكون شرطًا، ويختلف الأداء باختلاف صفة الوقت صحةً وكراهةً، فيكون سببًا للوجوب، وتقديم المشروط على الشرط جائز إذا كان الشرط شرطًا للوجوب كما في حولان الحول للزكاة وأما إذا كان الشرط شرطًا أي لوجوب الأداء للجواز لا يصح التقديم عليه كسائر شرائط الصلاة، وتقديم المسبب على السبب لا يجوز أصلاً، وههنا لما اجتمعت الشرطية والسببية، فلا جرم أن لا يجوز التقديم على الوقت.
كالوقت للصلاة أي في الوقت أي في الصلاة أي شرطية الجواز أي تقديم الصلاة ههنا شيئان: نفس الوجوب، ووجوب الأداء، فنفس الوجوب سببه الحقيقي هو الإيجاب القديم، وسببه الظاهري وهو الوقت أقيم مقامه، ووجوب الأداء السبب الحقيقي سببه الحقيقي إذا أدى إلخ إنما قيد به؛ لأن الصلاة إذا أديت بالإفراط بحيث تستغرق الوقت، فلا يكون الوقت فاضلاً. Statement of what the command entails regarding the ruling of time. And bound by it, i.e., the second [type] is a command bound by time, and it is of four types; because either the time is a container for the performed act, a condition for performance, and a cause for obligation—that is the first type. By 'container' is meant that it is not a measure for it, but rather exceeds it. By 'condition' is meant that the commanded act is not valid before its existence and is lost by its passing. By 'cause' is meant that this time has an effect on the obligation of the commanded act, even though the true effective agent in everything is Allah Most High, but obligation is attributed in appearance to the time; because in every moment a favor from Allah Most High reaches the servant, and this necessitates gratitude at every hour. However, these specific times are singled out for worship due to their greatness, the renewal of favors in them, and to avoid hardship in earning a livelihood if the time were entirely occupied with worship. Like the time of prayer.

(From al-Qamar) 'the performed act' etc.: The performed act is the form resulting from the specific pillars occurring in the time, such as standing, sitting, bowing, and prostrating in prayer. Performance is bringing it from the concealment of non-existence into the arena of existence, as it is said. (From al-Qamar) 'for obligation': i.e., for the very obligation, for the obligation of performance is by the command. And 'cause' according to them is what is a signifier of the realization of the effect and leads to its existence, as it is said. (From al-Qamar) 'What is meant' etc.: By way of contrast with the measure. The measure is that in which the commanded act occurs and by which it is measured, so it increases with the length of time and decreases with its shortness. (From al-Qamar) 'Rather exceeds it': i.e., exceeds the performed act, in that it encompasses that obligatory performed act and other things. (From al-Qamar) 'Because in every moment' etc.: With this explanation, what might be imagined of an appropriate connection between causes and effects is repelled, for there is no appropriate connection between time and the obligation of worship, so how can time be suitable as a cause for worship? (From al-Qamar) 'And only specified' etc.: Repelling an anticipated objection: that gratitude should be at every hour, so why were these five specific times singled out? (From al-Qamar) 'And the renewal of favors in them': For waking at dawn is life after sleep which is like death, so out of gratitude for it the dawn prayer was prescribed. Then during the day, when means of livelihood are obtained from foods, drinks, and other things, the noon prayer was prescribed out of gratitude. Then since there is a sort of sleep and rest after the noon prayer according to the habit of most people, the afternoon prayer was prescribed to compensate for heedlessness of the remembrance of Allah. Then when the favors of the day are completed, the sunset prayer was prescribed out of gratitude. And the prescription of the night prayer is for completing gratitude and improving the final outcome, and sleep after it is like dying upon faith and obedience. Thus mentioned the investigators. (From al-Qamar) 'And so that it does not lead' etc.: This does not suffice for anything, for it necessitates specifying times for worship because total occupation of time would cause hardship, but it does not necessitate specifying these five specific times. (From al-Qamar)

[PAGE_150]
Section on the Specific
149
Statement of what the command entails regarding the ruling of time. For the time in it exceeds performance if performed according to the Sunnah without excess, so it is a container. The time, and performance is not valid before the entry of the time and is lost by its passing, so it is a condition. And performance varies according to the quality of the time in terms of validity and dislikedness, so it is a cause for obligation. And preposing the conditional upon the condition is permissible when the condition is a condition for obligation, as in the passing of the year for zakat. But if the condition is a condition for permissibility, preposing upon it is not valid, like other conditions of prayer. And preposing the effect upon the cause is absolutely not permissible. Here, since conditionality and causality are combined, inevitably preposing upon the time is not permissible.

Like the time for prayer: i.e., regarding the time, i.e., in prayer, i.e., conditionality of permissibility, i.e., preposing the prayer. Here there are two things: the very obligation and the obligation of performance. So the true cause of the very obligation is the eternal determination, and its apparent cause—the time—is set in its place. And the obligation of performance: its true cause... 'if performed' etc. It is restricted because if the prayer is performed with excess such that it occupies the entire time, then the time is not exceeding. (From al-Qamar) 'And performance varies' etc.: For it is perfectly valid in its optimal time, disliked in disliked times, and invalid outside its time. The principle: that it varies according to the cause, so the time is a cause for the obligation of prayer and its binding upon the person's liability. (From al-Qamar) 'And performance varies' etc.: i.e., in the complete time the performance is complete, and in the deficient time it is deficient. (From al-Sanabili) And preposing the conditional upon the condition etc.: Repelling an anticipated objection: that since time is a condition for performance, then performance should be valid before the time, for preposing the conditional upon the condition is permissible. Do you not see that the passing of the year is a condition for the obligation of paying zakat, and it is permissible to give zakat before it? (From al-Qamar) 'Permissible'—this is not correct; the truth is that preposing the thing upon itself is invalid. And regarding zakat, the year is not a condition for obligation or for performance, and its being preceded is inconceivable. Thus said Ibn al-Malik. (From al-Qamar) 'Like other conditions of prayer': such as purity of garment, body, place, and others, for preposing the performance of prayer upon them is not permissible. (From al-Qamar) 'Preposing is not permissible' etc.: Because conditionality for permissibility and absolute causality both prevent preposing, so the impermissibility of preposing is firmly established. (From al-Sanabili) 'The very obligation' etc.: The difference between them: that the very obligation is the occupation of the responsible person's liability with the thing, and the obligation of performance is the necessity of discharging the liability from what is attached to it, so there must be a prior right in his liability. (From al-Sanabili) 'It is the eternal determination': Thus in al-Talwih. But the truth is contrary to that, for the eternal determination is the address of Allah Most High relating to the acts of the responsible persons, and it is the meaning of the attachment of the request to the act, so it is a cause for the obligation of performance, not for the very obligation. So the true cause of the very obligation is either the favors that Allah Most High has bestowed upon His servants, as some said, or Allah Most High as the commentator said previously. Unless one says it is a looseness in expression, and what is meant by 'eternal determination' is the Eternal Determiner, i.e., Allah Most High. So reflect. (From al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the various ways time can relate to a commanded act in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by stating that a command bound by time (الأمر المقيد بالوقت) has four types, but focuses on the first type where time simultaneously serves as a container (ظرف) for the performed act, a condition (شرط) for its performance, and a cause (سبب) for its obligation. He clarifies each term: 'container' means the time exceeds the act and is not its measure; 'condition' means the act is invalid before that time and is lost if the time passes; 'cause' means the time has an effect on the obligation of the act, though the true cause is Allah. The author then uses the example of prayer times to explain why specific times were chosen: the renewal of divine favors at those moments (e.g., waking from sleep like resurrection) and the need to avoid hardship from continuous worship. The commentary from al-Qamar and others expands on these points, addressing potential objections: e.g., why five specific times rather than constant gratitude? The answer is that favors are renewed at those times. Another objection: if time is a condition for performance, why can't the act be performed before the time, since preposing a conditional upon a condition is permissible (like zakat before the year)? The author distinguishes between a condition for obligation (شَرط الوجوب) and a condition for permissibility (شَرط الجواز). A condition for permissibility does not allow preposing, e.g., purity for prayer. In the case of prayer, time is both a condition for permissibility (since prayer before time is invalid) and a cause for obligation. Therefore, preposing is impermissible. The passage also discusses the cause of the very obligation (نفس الوجوب) vs. the obligation of performance (وجوب الأداء). The true cause of the very obligation is debated: some say it is the favors of Allah, others say it is Allah Himself (or the eternal determination as a loose expression for Allah). The obligation of performance is caused by the command (الإيجاب القديم). This is a nuanced usul discussion typical of Hanafi works.

Key Terms
الظرف (al-zarf): الوقت الذي يفضل عن المؤدى وليس معيارًا له (time that exceeds the performed act and is not its measure).
الشرط (al-shart): ما لا يصح المأمور به قبل وجوده ويفوت بفوته (condition without which the commanded act is invalid and is lost if the condition passes).
السبب (al-sabab): ما يكون معرفًا لتحقيق المسبب ومفضيًا إلى وجوده (what indicates the realization of the effect and leads to its existence).
شرط الوجوب (shart al-wujub): Condition for obligation: a condition whose existence triggers the obligation of the act. Preposing the act is permissible.
شرط الجواز (shart al-jawaz): Condition for permissibility/validity: a condition without which the act is invalid. Preposing the act is impermissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi usul, if time is both a condition for the validity of an act and a cause for its obligation (as in prayer), what is the ruling on performing the act before the time?
A) Permissible, because the time is a condition and preposing the conditional on the condition is allowed.
B) Impermissible, because the time is a condition for permissibility and a cause, both of which prevent preposing.
C) Permissible only if the act can be repeated later.
D) Impermissible only if the act is fasting; for prayer it is allowed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when time serves as both a condition for validity and a cause for obligation, preposing the act is impermissible.

Specification of Intention in Ramadan Fasting
Usul analysis of intention specification in fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on specification of intention in Ramadan fasting and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using qiyas.

Core Idea
Ramadan's exclusive time makes absolute intention suffice; no need to specify 'Ramadan'.

This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary addresses a key point of dispute: must one explicitly intend 'Ramadan' when fasting in that month? The author presents the Hanafi position, the Shafi'i analogy to prayer, and Zufar's extreme view, then affirms the middle path. Understanding this debate clarifies how the Hanafi school uses the concept of 'time exclusivity' to limit the need for specification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State clearly that the Hanafi school holds intention is necessary but ta'yin (specification) is not required for Ramadan fasting. The absolute intention to fast suffices.
This is the mufta bihi view.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-argument
Al-Shafi'i uses qiyas to prayer: just as prayer requires specifying which prayer (e.g., Zuhr vs. 'Asr), fasting requires specifying which fast. Identify the illa (effective cause) he assumes.
The illa is that the time is suitable for multiple acts.
3
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response distinguishes prayer time from Ramadan time. Prayer times (e.g., the time of Zuhr) can accommodate supererogatory prayers, but Ramadan is exclusively for the obligatory fast. Thus the qiyas is invalid due to a difference in the illa.
This is an example of fasid al-i'tibar (invalid analogy).
4
Consider Zufar's radical view and the middle path
Zufar reportedly held that even intention is unnecessary because the day is predetermined. The author rejects this as extremism and affirms the middle path: intention is required, but specification is not.
5
Derive the ruling from the proof
Using the text 'When Sha'ban ends, no fasting except for Ramadan', apply the usul principle that a general command (amm) restricts the time. Then the Hanafi rule that absolute intention suffices for a specified time applies.
This demonstrates istinbat (derivation).

وهو فيما قلنا فيصاب بمطلق الاسم، ومع الخطأ في الوصف تفريع على ما سبق أي فيصاب صوم رمضان بمطلق اسم الصوم بأن يقول: "نويت الصوم"، ومع الخطأ في الوصف
إذا انسلخ هذا المتن أورده العلي القاري في شرح مختصر "المنار" وأستاذ أساتذة الهند له في الصبح الصادق " . (القمر) لابد إلخ لئلا يلزم الجبر في صفة العبادة بأن يكون إمساك العبد على قصد آية قربة كانت للعبادة المفروضة شاء العبد أو أبى ونحن نقول إن الإطلاق في المتعين تعيين فلم لم يشرع في الوقت إلا الصوم الفرض ونوى مطلق الصوم، فتعين الفرض فحصل التعيين بإطلاق النية ونظيره ما إذا كان في الدار زيد وحده، وقلت يا إنسان تعين وهو للنداء، وطلب الإقبال فكذا ههنا. (القمر) لأنه متعين إلخ فكل إمساك يقع في نهار رمضان للصحيح المقيم يقع عن الصوم الفرض وإن لم ينو، وقلنا: إن هذا يكون جبرًا، والشرع عين الإمساك الذي هو قربة لصوم رمضان ولا قربة بدون النية. ثم اعلم أن الكرخي قال: من حكى هذا المذهب عن زفر فقد أخطأ، إنما قال زفر إن صوم جميع الشهر يصح بنية واحدة، وقال أبو اليسر : إن هذا القول قول زفر قاله في صغره ثم رجع عنه كذا في "الدراية" . (القمر) وهو إلخ: أي الأوسط في مذهبنا من أنه لا بد من النية، ولا يحتاج إلى التعيين. (القمر) ومع الخطأ إلخ: فإن الوقت ليس بصالح للوصف، بل إنما يقبل الأصل؛ لكونه متعينًا من الله تعالى، فالوصف لا يكون مشروعًا في ذلك الوقت فيبطل،
وليس من ضرورة بطلان الوصف بطلان الأصل، فبقي إطلاق أصل الصوم وبه يحصل الفرض (القمر) على ما سبق: أي على قول المصنف فيصير إلخ. (القمر) ومع الخطأ في الوصف الخ: لأنه نوى الأصل والوصف، والوقت قابل للأصل دون الوصف، فبطل الوصف وبقي إطلاق أصل الصوم. (القمر)
هذا المتن ،معروف، وقد أورده علي القاري أيضًا، و لم أجده في كتب الحديث الحاضرة عندي. [إشراق الأبصار ۸]

مبحث الخاص

بيان موجب الأمر في حكم الوقت
أيضًا بأن ينوي النفل أو واجبا آخر فلا يكون إلا عن رمضان والمراد بهذا الخطأ: ضد
كالقضاء والنذر
الصواب لا ضد العمد ؛ فإن العامد والمخطئ سواء في هذا الحكم.
إلا في المسافر ينوي واجبًا آخر عند أبي حنيفة ـد أبي حنيفة حله استثناء من مقدر أي يصاب رمضان مع الخطأ في الوصف في حق كل واحد إلا في المسافر حال كونه ينوي في رمضان واجبًا آخر القضاء والكفارة، فإنه يقع عما نوى لا عن رمضان عند رمضان عند أبي حنيفة أبي حنيفة رحله؛ لأن وجوب الأداء لما سقط في حقه يتخير بعد ذلك بين الأكل وبين واجب آخر، وعندهما لا يصح؛ لأن شهود الشهر موجود في حقه كالمقيم، وإنما رخص له بالإفطار لليسر، فإذا لم يترخص
من
عاد حكمه إلى الأصل، فلا يقع عما نوى بل عن رمضان، وهذا المسافر متلبس.
والمراد بهذا الخ: دفع وهم وهو: أن الظاهر من الخطأ في قول المصنف: إنه لو نوى النفل أو واجبًا آخر في رمضان عمدًا يكون عن رمضان ودفعه ظاهر. (السنبلي) ضد الصواب فالصواب في رمضان أن يصوم عن
ومع
رمضان لا عن غيره، فإذا نوى غيره نفلاً أو واجبًا ،آخر ، فقد أخطأ عمداً كان هذه النية أو خطأ. (القمر) استثناء إلخ: دفع دخل تقريره: أن الظاهر أنه استثناء من قوله فيصاب مطلق الاسم وهو لا يصح من وجوه: الأول: أن المستثنى ليس من جنس المستثنى منه والثاني: أن المستثنى منه ليس بمتعدد، وكلاهما لازم في المستثنى المتصل الذي هو الأصل، والثالث: أنه يلزم منه أن المسافر لا يصاب بمطلق الاسم، والواقع خلاف ذلك، فإنه لو نوى مطلق الصوم أجزأه من الفرض، فأجاب بما ترى وخلاصته أن الاستثناء متعلق بقوله: الخطأ في الوصف لا بقوله: بمطلق الاسم. (السنبلي حال كونه إلخ إيماء إلى أن قوله : ينوي إلخ حال من المسافر، وفيه أن الحال عن المفعول فيه غير معروف، اللهم إلا أن يقال: إنه حال عن الضمير في المسافر ؛ إذ المعنى إلا في الذي سافر، فالألف واللام ،موصول ولك أن تقول: أن الألف واللام في المسافر للعهد الذهني، فصح يوصف بالجملة، فقوله: ينوى إلى صفة للمسافر. (القمر) وعندهما إلخ بيان فائدة التقييد في المتن بقوله: عند أبي حنيفة . (القمر) وعندهما إلخ هما يقولان: إن شرع الصوم عام في حق المقيم والمسافر؛ لأن وجوبه لشهود الشهر وقد تحقق في حقه كما في حق المقيم، ولهذا لو صام عن فرض الوقت يجزئه، فلا يبقى غير صوم الوقت مشروعا في حق المسافر أيضا،
ولأبي حنيفة له في جواب قولهما طريقان إن شئت الاطلاع بهما ، فطالع التحقيق" شرح الحسامي". (السنبلي) لليسر: وهذه الرخصة التي لليسر لا تجوز أن يجعل غير صوم رمضان مشروعًا فيه، فإذا لم يترخص إلخ. (القمر) وهذا المسافر متلبس: إنما زاد هذا إيماء إلى أن قول المصنف بخلاف المريض ظرف مستقر . (القمر)
Section on the Specific (al-Khass) - Explanation of the implication of the command regarding the ruling of time. He then branched out on it being a standard, saying: So other than it becomes negated, i.e., since the month of Ramadan is a standard for fasting (i.e., not legislated), so other than the obligatory becomes negated in Ramadan, as Ali said: 'When Sha'ban ends, there is no fasting except for Ramadan, and the intention of specification is not required such that one says: 'I intend tomorrow to fast the obligatory of Ramadan' because specification was only legislated in prayer because its time is a container suitable for other than it as well, and that is absent here. Al-Shafi'i said: Specification of intention is necessary, by analogy to prayer. Zufar said: There is no need even for the original intention, because it is specifically determined by Allah's determination, and the best of affairs is the middle one. That is what we said: so it is attained by the absolute name, and with a mistake in the description, a branching on what preceded, i.e., the fast of Ramadan is attained by the absolute name of fasting such that one says: 'I intended fasting', and with a mistake in the description. This text was narrated by Ali al-Qari in his commentary on Mukhtasar al-Manar, and the teacher of the teachers of India has it in al-Subh al-Sadiq. (al-Qamar) 'It is necessary' etc. to avoid coercion in the attribute of worship, that the servant's restraint be intended as an act of worship that was for the obligatory worship, whether the servant wills or not. And we say: The absolute in what is specified is specification. So why was nothing legislated in the time except the obligatory fast, and he intended absolute fasting, then the obligatory becomes specified, so specification is obtained by the absolute intention, and its analogy is if Zayd was alone in the house and you said 'O person', it specifies him, and it is for calling and seeking attention, so likewise here. (al-Qamar) 'Because it is specified' etc., so every restraint that occurs in the day of Ramadan for a healthy resident occurs for the obligatory fast even if he did not intend. And we said: This would be coercion, and the Law specified restraint as an act of worship for the fast of Ramadan, and there is no act of worship without intention. Then know that al-Karkhi said: Whoever attributed this opinion to Zufar has erred; Zufar only said that fasting the entire month is valid with one intention. Abu al-Yusr said: This opinion is Zufar's, he said it in his youth then retracted it, as in al-Diraya. (al-Qamar) 'It is' etc.: i.e., the middle path in our madhhab: that intention is necessary, and specification is not needed. (al-Qamar) 'And with the mistake' etc.: For the time is not suitable for the description, rather it only accepts the original because it is specified by Allah, so the description is not legislated in that time and becomes invalid, and it is not necessary that the invalidity of the description entails the invalidity of the original, so the absolute original fasting remains and by it the obligatory is obtained. (al-Qamar) 'On what preceded': i.e., on the author's statement 'so other than it becomes negated' etc. (al-Qamar) 'And with the mistake in the description' etc.: Because he intended the original and the description, and the time is receptive to the original but not the description, so the description becomes invalid and the absolute original fasting remains. (al-Qamar) This text is well-known, and Ali al-Qari also cited it, and I did not find it in the hadith books available to me. [Ishraq al-Absar 8] Section on the Specific (al-Khass) - Explanation of the implication of the command regarding the ruling of time. Also, that he intends supererogatory or another obligatory (like makeup and vow) and it is not except from Ramadan. And the meaning of 'mistake' here is the opposite of correct, not the opposite of intentional; for the intentional and the mistaken are equal in this ruling. Except for a traveler who intends another obligatory (fast) according to Abu Hanifa - an exception from something implied, i.e., Ramadan is attained with a mistake in the description for everyone except a traveler when he intends in Ramadan another obligatory (makeup or expiation), then it falls on what he intended, not on Ramadan, according to Abu Hanifa. Because the obligation of performance has fallen from him, so he chooses thereafter between eating and another obligatory. According to the two companions, it is not valid because witnessing the month exists in his right as it does for a resident, and he was only given a concession to break the fast for ease; if he does not avail himself of the concession, his ruling returns to the original, so it does not fall on what he intended but on Ramadan. And this traveler is engaged in (fasting). The meaning of this is: Refuting a misunderstanding: that the apparent from 'mistake' in the author's statement is that if he intentionally intended supererogatory or another obligatory in Ramadan, it would be for Ramadan, and its refutation is apparent. (al-Sunbuli) 'Opposite of correct': the correct in Ramadan is to fast for Ramadan not for other than it. So if he intended other than it, supererogatory or another obligatory, he has made a mistake whether this intention was intentional or mistaken. (al-Qamar) 'Exception' etc.: Refuting an objection. Its explanation: The apparent is that it is an exception from his statement 'it is attained by the absolute name', and that is not valid for several reasons: First, the excepted is not of the same genus as the thing excepted from. Second, the thing excepted from is not multiple, and both are necessary in the connected exception which is the basic form. Third, it would entail that the traveler is not attained by the absolute name, but the reality is otherwise, for if he intended absolute fasting it would suffice for the obligatory. So he answered as you see, and the gist is that the exception relates to his statement 'mistake in the description' not to his statement 'by the absolute name'. (al-Sunbuli) 'At the time of being' etc. is an indication that his statement 'he intends' etc. is a circumstantial accusative referring to the traveler. And in it, the circumstantial accusative from the object of a preposition is not well-known, except if it is said that it is a circumstantial accusative from the pronoun in 'traveler', for the meaning is 'except for the one who traveled', so the 'alif lam' is a relative pronoun. And you could say: The 'alif lam' in 'traveler' is for mental reference, so it is correct to describe it with a clause, so his statement 'he intends' is a description of the traveler. (al-Qamar) 'And according to them' etc.: Explanation of the benefit of the restriction in the text by his statement 'according to Abu Hanifa'. (al-Qamar) 'And according to them' etc.: They (the two companions) say: The law of fasting is general in respect of the resident and the traveler, because its obligation is due to witnessing the month and that has been realized in his right as in the right of the resident, and for this reason if he fasts for the obligatory of the time it suffices him, so no other than the fast of the time remains legislated in the right of the traveler either. For Abu Hanifa, in response to their statement, there are two ways; if you wish to examine them, consult al-Tahqiq, Sharh al-Husami. (al-Sunbuli) 'For ease': This concession for ease does not permit making other than the fast of Ramadan legislated in it. So if he does not avail himself of the concession, etc. (al-Qamar) 'And this traveler is engaged': He added this only as an indication that the author's statement 'contrary to the sick' is a stable adverb phrase. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn dealing with the rulings of intention in fasting Ramadan. The central issue is whether one needs to specify (ta'yin) that one is fasting Ramadan when making the intention. The Hanafi position is that specification is not required; an absolute intention to fast suffices because the time (Ramadan) is exclusively for the obligatory fast, so the absolute intention automatically specifies it. This is contrasted with prayer, where specification is needed because prayer times can accommodate various prayers. Al-Shafi'i analogizes to prayer and requires specification. Zufar reportedly went further, saying no intention at all is needed because the day is predetermined. The commentary explains that the correct Hanafi view is the middle path: intention is necessary but specification is not. It then addresses a scenario where one makes a mistake in the description (al-khata' fi al-wasf), e.g., intending a supererogatory or makeup fast during Ramadan. In such a case, the fast still counts as Ramadan for a resident, because the time accepts only the original obligatory fast. An exception is made for a traveler who intends another obligatory fast (like makeup or expiation) according to Abu Hanifa: it falls on what he intended. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, holding that it counts as Ramadan for the traveler as well. The commentary also clarifies that 'mistake' in this context means error (khata'), not intentional wrongdoing; the ruling is the same for both intentional and mistaken deviation. It further explains the grammatical structure of the exception and provides dialectical reasoning for the Hanafi position. Overall, this passage is a detailed usul-level discussion of how general rules of intention apply to a specific act of worship, demonstrating Hanafi methodology in deriving rulings.

Key Terms
النية (al-niyyah): The intention in the heart to perform an act of worship.
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Explicitly specifying which act of worship one intends (e.g., 'I intend the obligatory fast of Ramadan').
الخطأ في الوصف (al-khata' fi al-wasf): Intending a description of the act that does not match the actual time (e.g., intending a supererogatory fast in Ramadan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab as explained in this passage, what is the ruling if a resident in Ramadan makes an absolute intention to fast (e.g., 'I intend to fast') without specifying that it is for Ramadan?
A) The fast is invalid because specification is required.
B) The fast is valid and counts as the obligatory Ramadan fast.
C) The fast is valid but counts as a supererogatory fast.
D) The fast is only valid if he later specifies it as Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that intention is required but specification is not; the exclusivity of Ramadan time makes absolute intention sufficient for the obligatory fast.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
9 min
Mistake in Description: Intending Another Fast During Ramadan
Usul analysis of mistake in description in Ramadan fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on mistake in description (khata' fi al-wasf) to differentiate between a resident and a traveler who intends another obligatory fast in Ramadan, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
A mistake in description does not invalidate the fast except for a traveler intending another obligatory fast per Abu Hanifa.

This passage continues the discussion on intention in Ramadan by addressing a scenario where someone intends a supererogatory or makeup fast instead of Ramadan. The author explains that for a resident, the fast still counts as Ramadan, but for a traveler, there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions. Understanding this ruling is crucial for fatwa on missed intentions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of 'mistake in description'
The passage defines 'mistake' as intending a description other than the correct one (e.g., supererogatory instead of obligatory). This includes both intentional and unintentional errors.
The term 'khata' here includes 'amd (intentional) as well, because the ruling is the same.
2
State the general ruling for a resident
For a healthy resident who intends supererogatory or another obligatory fast in Ramadan, the fast counts as the obligatory Ramadan fast. The time accepts only the original, so the description is invalid but the original remains.
This is the default ruling for all non-travelers.
3
Identify the exception for a traveler
According to Abu Hanifa, if a traveler intends another obligatory fast (qada, kaffara) in Ramadan, it falls on what he intended, not on Ramadan. The reason: the traveler has a concession to break his fast, so he is not committed to Ramadan's exclusivity.
This exception applies only to another obligatory fast, not to supererogatory.
4
Analyze the disagreement of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that even a traveler's fast counts as Ramadan, because witnessing the month is still present. The concession to break does not change the nature of the time.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text presents Abu Hanifa's view as the one discussed, but does not explicitly state tarjih. Later Hanafi practice usually follows the Sahibayn in many issues, but here the matn seems to adopt Abu Hanifa's view with 'according to Abu Hanifa'.
