(al-Qamar) And it is not meant that the prohibition of sensual acts is sensory not dependent on revelation, because prohibition is a ruling, and rulings with us are established by revelation, not by any other proof. (al-Qamar) When unrestricted: it is only qualified with this because the prohibition qualified by a contextual indication falls on what the indication requires, whether it is a prohibition of sensual acts or of legal acts. (al-Qamar) Falls etc. because intrinsic evil is the original evil, so it is the primary connotation of prohibition when unrestricted. (al-Qamar) Unless evidence arises: and it is not hidden from you that the falling of the prohibition of sensual acts on intrinsic evil is conditioned by it being unrestricted and without preventives, so it does not include cases where evidence to the contrary arises, thus it is not correct to exclude it by saying 'unless evidence arises'? Unless one says that the exception is discontinuous. (al-Qamar)

Commentary
This passage begins a new section after the division of prohibition, explaining a key Hanafi principle: when the Lawgiver prohibits a 'sensual act' (fī'l ḥissī)—an act whose physical reality existed before revelation—the default assumption is that the prohibition indicates its intrinsic evil (qabīḥ li-aynihi). Examples include killing, adultery, and wine-drinking; their essences remain unchanged by the Shari'a. The author clarifies that this does not mean their prohibition is known by sensory perception—rather, all rulings come from revelation. The principle applies when the prohibition is stated without qualification and without contrary evidence. If a contextual indication points to extrinsic evil, the prohibition is interpreted accordingly. The passage also provides a definition of zina (adultery) from Majma' al-Barakat, distinguishing it from cases with semblance of ownership or marriage. Finally, the gloss notes a potential logical issue: if the rule is that prohibition of sensual acts falls on intrinsic evil only when unrestricted, then stating 'unless evidence arises' may be an exception that actually breaks the rule—unless it is discontinuous exception (istithnā' munqaṭi').

Key Terms
الأفعال الحسية (al-afʿāl al-ḥissiyya): Acts whose physical essence is known prior to revelation and remains unchanged after revelation, e.g., killing, adultery, drinking wine.
قبح لعينه (qabīḥ li-aynihi): Intrinsic evil: the act is evil in its essence regardless of external circumstances, so prohibition indicates its inherent badness.

D) The act is commonly known as evil among all people.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi default rule: prohibition of a sensual act without qualification or preventives indicates intrinsic evil (qabīḥ li-aynihi).

Essential vs. Descriptive Badness (Qabh) in Hanafi Usul
Defines qabh and divides it into essential and non-essential, applied to sensual and shar'i acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a prohibition on a shar'i act implies descriptive badness (qabh wasfi) or essential badness (qabh li-aynihi) and apply the Hanafi default rule that an unrestricted prohibition on shar'i acts indicates descriptive badness unless evidence shows otherwise.

Core Idea
A prohibition on a shar'i act at first implies descriptive badness, not invalidity—only if the act is inherently bad does it invalidate.

In Hanafi usul, not all prohibitions are equal. Some prohibitions make an act invalid; others merely make it disliked or forbidden without corrupting the act itself. This lesson unpacks the key distinction between essential badness (qabh li-aynihi) and descriptive badness (qabh wasfi), focusing on how this applies to the acts prescribed and transformed by the Shari'ah. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting the legal effect of a prohibition (nahy) on acts like prayer, fasting, or sale.

Guided Path
1
Define qabh and its two types
Extract from the passage the definition of qabh as 'harm' and its division into essential (li-aynihi) and descriptive (li-ghayrihi/wasfi). Note that descriptive badness is also called 'wasfi' because it attaches to a description of the act.
Essential badness is intrinsic; descriptive badness comes from an external factor.
2
Apply to sensory acts: example of intercourse during menses
The passage explains that the prohibition on intercourse during menstruation is qabh li-ghayrihi because it is a sensory act and the harm (adha) is external, proven by Qur'an 2:222.
Sensory acts are those whose meanings are clear from custom; their prohibition does not change their nature.
3
Apply to shar'i acts: the default rule
Shar'i acts are those whose original linguistic meanings were expanded by the Law (e.g., prayer from supplication to a ritual; fasting from abstention to a regulated worship). For these, an unrestricted prohibition (nahy) is interpreted as qabh wasfi – i.e., the prohibition attaches to a description, not the essence.
This is the Hanafi default: no invalidity unless proven otherwise.
4
Identify exceptions: where evidence indicates essential badness
The passage lists exceptions: if clear evidence (dalil) shows that a shar'i act is bad in its essence, then the prohibition is essential and invalidates the act. Examples given: selling al-Madamin (unborn offspring) and al-Malaqih (fetuses), and praying without ritual purity.
These exceptions require a specific qarina (indication) that the act is inherently corrupt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص
بيان أقسام القبح
وهو الأذى كالوطء حالة الحيض حرام لغيره مع أنه فعل حسي لقيام الدليل. وعن الأمور الشرعية يقع على الذي اتصل به وصفا عطف على قوله: عن الأفعال الحسية أي والنهي عن الأمور الشرعية يقع على القسم الذي اتصل به القبح وصفًا يعني يحمل على أنه قبيح لغيره، وصفا والمراد بالأمور الشرعية: ما تغيرت معانيها الأصلية بعد ورود الشرع بها كالصوم والصلاة، والبيع والإجارة، فإن الصوم: هو الإمساك في الأصل، وزيدت عليه في الشرع أشياء. والصلاة: هو الدعاء زيدت عليه أشياء. والبيع مبادلة المال بالمال فقط زيدت عليها أهلية العاقدين، ومحلية المعقود عليه، وغير ذلك. والإجارة: مبادلة المال بالمنافع زيدت عليه معلومية المستأجر، والأجرة، والمدة وغير ذلك، فالنهي عن هذه الأفعال عند الإطلاق يحمل على القبح الوصفي، إلا إذا أي الأفعال الشرعية أي وقت عدم القرينة دل الدليل على كونه قبيحًا لعينه كالنهي عن بيع المضامين والملاقيح، وصلاة المحدث.
Section: The Specific. Explanation of the divisions of badness. It is harm, like intercourse during menstruation, forbidden due to something else, even though it is a sensory act because of evidence. And concerning religious matters, it falls on that which is connected to it descriptively, as a conjunction to his statement: 'concerning sensory acts' i.e., and the prohibition of religious matters falls on the category to which badness is connected descriptively, meaning it is interpreted as being bad due to something else, descriptively. And by 'religious matters' is meant those whose original meanings changed after the advent of the Law, such as fasting, prayer, sale, and hire. For fasting is originally abstention, and things were added to it in the Law. Prayer is supplication, things were added to it. Sale is exchange of property for property only, and the capacity of the contracting parties, the eligibility of the object of contract, and other things were added to it. Hire is exchange of property for usufruct, and knowledge of the hirer, the wage, the duration, and other things were added to it. So the prohibition of these acts when unrestricted is interpreted as descriptive badness, unless—i.e., in the case of religious acts when absent the presumption—evidence indicates it is bad in its essence, like the prohibition of selling al-Madamin and al-Malaqih, and the prayer of one who is ritually impure.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the types of qabh (badness/ugliness) as they relate to prohibitions. The author begins by dividing qabh into two categories: badness due to something else (qabh li-ghayrihi) and badness in essence (qabh li-aynihi). The example given for the first is intercourse during menstruation: it is a sensory act (hissi) but prohibited due to the external harm (adha). The proof is Qur'an 2:222. Then the discussion shifts to 'amr shar'i (religious acts) like prayer, fasting, sale, etc. For these, when a prohibition is stated without qualification, it is interpreted as descriptive badness (qabh wasfi), meaning the act is not inherently bad but the prohibition attaches to a description. However, if a specific evidence (dalil) shows that the act is bad in essence, then it is treated as such. Examples of essence-bad acts: selling al-Madamin (unborn offspring in loins) and al-Malaqih (fetuses in wombs), and praying without purity. The author also clarifies the definition of shar'i acts: they are actions whose original linguistically meanings were expanded by the Law. For instance, fasting originally meant abstaining, but legally includes conditions like time, intention, and avoidance of specific nullifiers. Similarly, sale requires capacity of parties and existence of subject matter. The key principle: for shar'i acts, prohibition at face value (atlaq) implies descriptive badness unless there is a counter-indication that points to essential badness.

Key Terms
القبح (al-qabḥ): Badness or ugliness that attaches to an act due to a prohibition
وصفي (waṣfī): Descriptive badness: the prohibition is attached to a description, not the essence of the act
مجاور (mujāwir): Adjoining badness: the badness is due to an external factor adjacent to the act

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what does an unrestricted prohibition (nahy) on a shar'i act (e.g., prayer or sale) imply?
A) The act is invalid (fasad) immediately.
B) The act is bad in essence (qabh li-aynihi).
C) The act has descriptive badness (qabh wasfi) and is not necessarily invalid.
D) The act is permissible (halal) but discouraged.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, an unrestricted prohibition on a shar'i act defaults to descriptive badness; invalidity requires evidence of essential badness.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i: Do Prohibitions on Worship Acts Imply Essential Badness?
Presents Hanafi proof that prohibition on shar'i acts implies descriptive badness, refuting Shafi'i's qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument that prohibition on shar'i acts implies only descriptive badness (qabh wasfi) and evaluate the Shafi'i qiyas to sensory acts.

Core Idea
A prohibition on a shar’i act implies descriptive badness, not essential badness, because the prohibition itself requires the act to exist as a possible object.

This lesson delves into a fundamental usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the effect of a prohibition (nahy) on a shar'i act, such as prayer or fasting. Does the prohibition automatically render the act invalid (fasid), or does it merely indicate that the act is undesirable while remaining legally possible? The passage presents the Hanafi position that badness is established by implication (iqtida'), so the prohibition cannot nullify its own implicator; the act must remain valid in principle. In contrast, the Shafi'i school analogizes from sensory acts to argue for essential badness. Understanding this disagreement is crucial for deriving rulings on acts like praying at prohibited times or with missing conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Dispute
State the disagreement: Shafi'i holds that prohibition of a shar'i act implies essential badness (qabh li-aynihi) and thus invalidity; Hanafi holds it implies only descriptive badness (qabh wasfi) without automatic invalidity.
Focus on the difference between essential and descriptive badness.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Proof: Iqtida'
Examine the Hanafi argument: badness is established by implication (iqtida'), so it must not nullify the implicator (the prohibition itself). The prohibition intends non-performance by choice, not impossibility.
The prohibition implies both badness and the possibility of the act's existence.
3
Evaluate the Shafi'i Qiyas
Consider the Shafi'i analogy to sensory acts (e.g., intercourse during menstruation). In sensory acts, prohibition implies essential badness due to external harm; Shafi'i extends this to shar'i acts.
Hanafi response: shar'i acts have different structures; their meanings are determined by law, not by harm alone.
4
Derive the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Apply the dispute to a concrete case: a prayer performed without wudu. Shafi'i may rule it invalid because the prohibition implies essential badness; Hanafi says invalidity comes from a separate condition (purity), not from the prohibition itself.
This illustrates the practical impact of the usul disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن القبح يثبت اقتضاء، فلا يتحقق على وجه يبطل به المقتضي وهو النهي دليل على الدعوى الأخيرة، وبيانه يقتضي بسطاً وهو: أن في النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية اختلافا، فقال الشافعي حبه: إنه يقتضي القبح لعينه وهو الكامل قياسًا على الأول على ما يأتي، ونحن نقول: إن النهي يراد به عدم الفعل مضافًا إلى اختيار العباد،
Because badness is established by implication (iqtida'), so it is not realized in a manner that nullifies the implicator (muqtadi), which is the prohibition. That is evidence for the last claim. Its explanation requires expansion: that there is a disagreement regarding the prohibition of religious acts. Al-Shafi'i (may he be pleased) said: It (the prohibition) implies badness in essence (li-aynihi), which is the perfect type, by analogy to the first (i.e., sensory acts) as will come. And we say: The prohibition intends non-performance of the act while annexed to the choice of the servants.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents the core Hanafi argument against the Shafi'i position on the effect of prohibitions on shar'i acts. The Hanafi claim is that for religious acts (like prayer, fasting, etc.), a prohibition does not automatically render the act invalid (fasad) because badness is established only by implication (iqtida'). That is, the prohibition implies badness, but also implies the possibility of the act's existence. If the prohibition were to nullify the act entirely (by making it invalid), then it would contradict the very implication (muqtadi) which is the prohibition itself—since the prohibition must have an object to apply to. Therefore, the prohibition cannot be interpreted as essential badness that would invalidate the act. Al-Shafi'i, on the other hand, analogizes from sensory acts: just as prohibition on sensory acts (like intercourse during menstruation) implies essential badness (the act is inherently undesirable due to an external harm), the same should apply to religious acts. But the Hanafi response is that the prohibition of religious acts is intended to encourage non-performance by choice, not to make the act itself impossible or invalid. The act remains legally possible but forbidden. The key point: the prohibition does not necessarily cause invalidity (fasid) for shar'i acts unless specific evidence shows badness in essence. This is a fundamental usul dispute between the schools.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtidā'): Implication: the quality of a prohibition that implies both the badness and the existence of the act (as a possible object of prohibition)
المقتضي (al-muqtaḍī): The implicator: that which requires something else; here, the prohibition itself is the implicator of badness.
القبح لعينه (al-qabḥ li-ʿaynihi): Essential badness: the act is inherently bad and thus invalid or impossible.
القبح الوصفي (al-qabḥ al-waṣfī): Descriptive badness: the act is bad but remains legally possible; prohibition does not automatically invalidate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i views on the prohibition of shar'i acts as presented in this passage?
A) Hanafi says prohibition implies essential badness; Shafi'i says descriptive.
B) Hanafi says prohibition implies descriptive badness; Shafi'i says essential.
C) Hanafi says prohibition invalidates the act; Shafi'i says it does not.
D) Both agree that prohibition always implies essential badness.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a prohibition on a shar'i act implies descriptive badness only; the act is not automatically invalid unless separate evidence indicates essential badness.

Distinction Between Qabih li-Aynihi and Qabih li-Ghayrihi
Classifying prohibitions and defending Abu Hanifa's position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qabih li-aynihi and qabih li-ghayrihi and apply the classification to transactional prohibitions to determine whether the act is fasid or batil.

Core Idea
A prohibition may target the essence or an external description; only the latter leaves the act's original validity intact.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, not all prohibited acts are treated equally. Some acts are foul in their very essence (qabih li-aynihi), while others are foul due to an extraneous condition or description (qabih li-ghayrihi). This lesson unpacks the classification as explained in the passage, focusing on examples like selling wine or conditional sales. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining whether a transaction is merely corrupt (fasid) or completely void (batil).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that qabih li-aynihi is an act intrinsically foul (e.g., selling a free person if considered so), while qabih li-ghayrihi is an act that is valid in essence but becomes prohibited due to an external description or condition.
The passage gives selling for wine as an example of qabih li-ghayrihi because the essence (exchange) is valid, but the condition (using wine as price) makes it corrupt.
2
Apply to selling for wine
Analyze the sale of an item for wine: the contract's essence (offer and acceptance) is valid, but the consideration (thaman) is wine, which is non-fungible (ghayr mutaqawwim). The prohibition attaches to the condition (the type of price), not the essence.
The passage states that wine is mal (property) but not mutaqawwim; therefore, the sale is fasid, not batil.
3
Apply to conditional sales
Consider a sale with a condition benefiting one party, e.g., the seller retains usage for a month. The essence is valid, but the condition creates a prohibited description. Hence, the sale is fasid due to qabih li-ghayrihi.
The passage lists conditions like 'that the seller uses the slave for a month'—these are extraneous descriptions.
4
Distinguish from qabih li-aynihi
Contrast with acts where the prohibition is on the essence itself, such as marrying a mahram (if the subject is absent). In Hanafi, many apparent qabih li-aynihi cases are reinterpreted as metaphorical negation.
This prepares for the next lesson on the objection.

وفيه نفع لأحد المتعاقدين، أو للمعقود عليه الذي هو أهل الاستحقاق والبيع بالخمر ونحوه كل ذلك مشروع باعتبار ذاته، وإنما الفساد باعتبار الشرط الزائد، فيكون مفيدًا للملك البيع الفاسد بعد القبض، وكذا صوم يوم النحر مشروع باعتبار كونه صومًا، وغير مشروع باعتبار أي قبض المشتري المبيع الوصف الذي هو الإعراض عن الضيافة، فتعلق النهي في كل ذلك بالوصف لا بالأصل. ثم ههنا سؤال مقدر على أبي حنيفة له وهو: أن بيع الحر، والمضامين، والملاقيح، ونكاح المحارم من الأفعال الشرعية مع أن ههنا لم يقع على القبح لغيره، بل على القبح لعينه عندكم كالأم وأم الأم وفيه نفع لأحد المتعاقدين للبائع كما إذا باع عبدًا على أن يستخدمه البائع شهرًا، أو دارًا على أن يسكنها، وللمشتري كما إذا اشترى ثوبًا على أن يخيطه البائع قميصا للمشتري. (القمر) هو أهل الاستحقاق أي من أهل أن يثبت له حق على الغير، ويقع منه الخصومة، وطلب الحق بأن يكون أدميا، وأما إذا لم يكن المعقود عليه من أهل الاستحقاق فلا ضرر فيه كما إذا باع فرسا بشرط أن يعلفه المشتري كل يوم كذا منا من الشعير. (القمر)
والبيع بالخمر: معطوف على المجرور في قوله كالبيع إلخ، ثم اعلم أن الخمر مال؛ لأن المال ما يميل إليه الطبع، ويدخر لوقت الحاجة، أو ما خلق لمصالح الآدمي، ويجري فيه الشح والضمنة والخمر كذلك، فصار مالاً، لكنه غير متقوم، فإن المتقوم ما يحل الانتفاع به شرعًا، والشارع منع عن تسليم الخمر وتسلمه، والانتفاع به، فصار غير متقوم، ففي البيع بالخمر جعل الخمر ثمنًا، وهو يصلح للثمنية؛ لكونه مالاً، فيصح البيع لكنه يمتنع تسليمه فجاء الخلل في هذا البيع من جهة الثمن، والثمن يكون غير مقصود، بل يكون ذريعة إلى المقصود، فإن المقصود هو المبيع، ولذا يشترط القدرة على المبيع، ولا يشترط القدرة على الثمن مع أن الانتفاع بالأعيان لا بالأثمان، فبهذا الاعتبار صار الثمن من جملة الأوصاف، والشروط، فجاء الخلل من الشرط، فصار هذا البيع بيعا فاسدًا لا باطلاً؛ لتحقق الركن، وهو الإيجاب والقبول الصادران من الأهل مضافًا إلى المحل وهو المبيع. (القمر) وهؤلاء: أي الحر، والمضامين والملاقيح ليسوا بمال، وقد مر حال مالية الحر فتذكر (القمر) وهن: أي المحارم محرمات ورده في الصبح الصادق : بأن نكاح المحارم نكاح حقيقة؛ لأن نكاحهن كان جائزا في الشرع السابق، وبالنسخ لا يبطل المحلية فالمحل قابل كيف؟ وأن النكاح ليس إلا لازدواج بين الرجل والمرأة لا غير. (القمر) ويمكن إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقرير الدخل: أن النهي لا يمكن أن يكون مجازا عن النسخ؛ لأن النسخ لابد له من وجود الناسخ والمنسوخ في شريعة واحدة و لم يجز بيع الحر في شريعة النبي الله أصلاً، وتقرير الرد ظاهر. (السنبلي)
Section on the Specific: Explanation of the types of foulness. Among them is benefit for one of the two contracting parties, or for the object of contract that is eligible to claim rights. And selling for wine and similar things: all of that is prescribed in terms of its essence, but corruption is due to an additional condition. So it becomes beneficial for ownership after the buyer takes possession, as does fasting on the day of Nahr being prescribed in terms of being a fast, and not prescribed in terms of the description which is turning away from hospitality. So the prohibition in all that attaches to the description, not to the essence. Then here there is an implied objection to Abu Hanifa, namely: that selling a free person, selling 'al-damin' and 'al-malaqih', and marriage to mahrams are among the prescribed acts, but here the prohibition does not fall on 'foul due to something else', rather on 'foul in itself' according to you, like the mother and the grandmother. And it contains benefit for one of the two parties... (continued) The author replied: The prohibition of selling a free person, al-damin, al-malaqih, and marrying mahrams is metaphorical for negation (nafy). So 'free person' is general for one free by origin or free by manumission. 'Al-damin' is plural of 'madmunah' meaning what is in the loins of fathers. 'Al-malaqih' is plural of 'maluqah' meaning what is in the wombs of mothers. 'Al-mahram' is general for prohibition by lineage or prohibition by marriage. In summary, the prohibition of these is interpreted as negation by way of metaphor. So it was abrogation due to absence of its subject... And it is possible that it is terminological abrogation according to those who say: lifting the original permissibility and lifting what was in Jahiliyyah or previous laws is called abrogation...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of prohibited acts (qabih) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It distinguishes between qabih li-aynihi (foul in itself) and qabih li-ghayrihi (foul due to something else). The author, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi or possibly the commentator (likely al-Qamar), explains that prohibitions in transactions such as selling wine or conditional sales attach to the description (e.g., the condition) not the essence. Therefore, the act remains legally valid in origin but becomes corrupt (fasid) due to the extraneous condition. The text then presents a potential objection against Imam Abu Hanifa: selling a free person, selling what is in loins and wombs, and marrying mahrams are considered prohibited in themselves by other schools, yet Abu Hanifa also considers them prohibited. How can he maintain that prohibitions on prescribed acts only fall on descriptions? The commentary answers by stating that these prohibitions are metaphorical (majaz) for outright negation (nafy). That is, such 'sales' or 'marriages' are null because they lack a valid subject (mahall): a free person is not property, and mahrams are not permissible spouses. Hence, the prohibition is not on a valid act but declares the act nonexistent. This is a core Hanafi position: many prohibitions that appear to be on acts are actually negations of the act's validity. The commentary also discusses the concept of abrogation (naskh) in this context, noting that the term is used metaphorically to mean lifting the original permissibility from previous laws.

Key Terms
قبيح لعينه (qabīḥ li-ʿaynihi): An act that is foul in its very essence, such as selling a free person or marrying a mahram according to other schools.
قبيح لغيره (qabīḥ li-ghayrihi): An act that is foul due to an extraneous description, not its essence, e.g., selling wine due to the condition of wine being used as price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is selling wine as a price considered qabih li-ghayrihi?
A) Because wine is inherently najis and cannot be sold.
B) Because the essence of the exchange is valid, but the description (wine as price) is prohibited.
C) Because the seller does not own the wine.
D) Because the contract lacks offer and acceptance.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of prohibitions into qabih li-aynihi and qabih li-ghayrihi determines whether an act is void (batil) or merely corrupt (fasid).

Metaphorical Prohibition as Negation: Hanafi Defense
Classifying prohibitions and defending Abu Hanifa's position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school interprets prohibitions on selling free persons and marrying mahrams as metaphorical negation (majaz 'an al-nafy) and distinguish this from qabih li-ghayrihi.

Core Idea
Prohibitions on acts that lack a valid subject are metaphors for negation, not genuine prohibitions on prescribed acts.

This lesson addresses a specific objection to Abu Hanifa: if prohibitions only attach to descriptions (qabih li-ghayrihi), how can selling a free person or marrying a mahram be prohibited when no description is involved? The Hanafi response introduces the concept of metaphorical prohibition (majaz) — the imperative of prohibition is actually used to convey negation because the act's subject is absent. We will trace the argument and its implications for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Recognize that opponents argue: selling a free person, selling fetuses in wombs (malaqih), and marrying mahrams are prohibited, yet they are prescribed acts. According to Abu Hanifa, prohibitions should only fall on descriptions, but here no description is involved.
The objection asserts that these acts are qabih li-aynihi (foul in themselves), contradicting the rule that prohibitions on prescribed acts are qabih li-ghayrihi.
2
Analyze the subject of the prohibition
Examine what the prohibition actually targets: for selling a free person, the 'sale' lacks a valid subject (a free person is not property). For marrying a mahram, the marriage lacks a permissible spouse. Thus, the act is not a valid act in itself.
The Hanafi claim: the prohibition is not on a valid act but declares the act nonexistent.
3
Introduce the concept of majaz 'an al-nafy
The prohibition (nahy) is a figurative use of language to convey negation (nafy). The imperative 'do not sell' is metaphorically equivalent to 'there is no valid sale'.
The passage states: 'والنهي عن بيع الحر ... مجاز عن النفي' — the prohibition is metaphorical for negation.
4
Relate to naskh
The passage also mentions that this negation can be considered abrogation (naskh) of the original permissibility from previous laws (e.g., selling free persons was allowed in the law of Yusuf).
This is not technical naskh but a metaphorical use of the term.

وفيه نفع لأحد المتعاقدين، أو للمعقود عليه الذي هو أهل الاستحقاق والبيع بالخمر ونحوه كل ذلك مشروع باعتبار ذاته، وإنما الفساد باعتبار الشرط الزائد، فيكون مفيدًا للملك البيع الفاسد بعد القبض، وكذا صوم يوم النحر مشروع باعتبار كونه صومًا، وغير مشروع باعتبار أي قبض المشتري المبيع الوصف الذي هو الإعراض عن الضيافة، فتعلق النهي في كل ذلك بالوصف لا بالأصل. ثم ههنا سؤال مقدر على أبي حنيفة له وهو: أن بيع الحر، والمضامين، والملاقيح، ونكاح المحارم من الأفعال الشرعية مع أن ههنا لم يقع على القبح لغيره، بل على القبح لعينه عندكم كالأم وأم الأم وفيه نفع لأحد المتعاقدين للبائع كما إذا باع عبدًا على أن يستخدمه البائع شهرًا، أو دارًا على أن يسكنها، وللمشتري كما إذا اشترى ثوبًا على أن يخيطه البائع قميصا للمشتري. (القمر) أو للمعقود عليه: أي المبيع كشرط أن لا يبيع المشتري العبد المشترى، فإن العبد يعجبه أن لا يتداوله الأيدي. (القمر) هو أهل الاستحقاق أي من أهل أن يثبت له حق على الغير، ويقع منه الخصومة، وطلب الحق بأن يكون أدميا، وأما إذا لم يكن المعقود عليه من أهل الاستحقاق فلا ضرر فيه كما إذا باع فرسا بشرط أن يعلفه المشتري كل يوم كذا منا من الشعير. (القمر)
والبيع بالخمر: معطوف على المجرور في قوله كالبيع إلخ، ثم اعلم أن الخمر مال؛ لأن المال ما يميل إليه الطبع، ويدخر لوقت الحاجة، أو ما خلق لمصالح الآدمي، ويجري فيه الشح والضمنة والخمر كذلك، فصار مالاً، لكنه غير متقوم، فإن المتقوم ما يحل الانتفاع به شرعًا، والشارع منع عن تسليم الخمر وتسلمه، والانتفاع به، فصار غير متقوم، ففي البيع بالخمر جعل الخمر ثمنًا، وهو يصلح للثمنية؛ لكونه مالاً، فيصح البيع لكنه يمتنع تسليمه فجاء الخلل في هذا البيع من جهة الثمن، والثمن يكون غير مقصود، بل يكون ذريعة إلى المقصود، فإن المقصود هو المبيع، ولذا يشترط القدرة على المبيع، ولا يشترط القدرة على الثمن مع أن الانتفاع بالأعيان لا بالأثمان، فبهذا الاعتبار صار الثمن من جملة الأوصاف، والشروط، فجاء الخلل من الشرط، فصار هذا البيع بيعا فاسدًا لا باطلاً؛ لتحقق الركن، وهو الإيجاب والقبول الصادران من الأهل مضافًا إلى المحل وهو المبيع. (القمر) ونحوه: كالبيع بالقيمة مع السكوت عن الثمن. (القمر) مشروع باعتبار إلخ فإن في الصوم أي الإمساك عن المفطرات الثلاثة مع النية، حصول التقوى كما قال : الله تعالى: ﴿ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَتَّقُونَ ﴾ (البقرة :(۲۱)، وفيه معرفة قدر النعم، وفيه انتفاء حرارة الشهوة.(القمر)
لم يقع: أي النهي على القبح لغيره، فبطل ما قلتم من أن النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية يقع على القبح لغيره وصفًا.(القمر)
فأجاب عنه المصنف وقال: والنهي عن بيع الحر، والمضامين والملاقيح، ونكاح المحارم مجاز عن النفي، فالحر عام من أن يكون حر الأصل، أو حر العتاقة. والمضامين جمع مضمونة، وهو ما في أصلاب الآباء والملاقيح جمع ملقوحة، وهو ما في أرحام الأمهات والمحارم عام من أن يكون حرمة القرابة أو حرمة المصاهرة وبالجملة فالنهي عن هؤلاء محمول على النفي بطريق المجاز ، فكان نسحًا لعدم محله أي فكان هذا النهي كله نسخا للمشروعية، أي إعداما وإبطالاً لعدم محل النهي؛ إذ محل البيع هو المال، وهؤلاء ليسوا بمال ومحل النكاح المحللات، وهنّ محرمات بالنص، وفي إيراد لفظ النسخ بعد النفي تنبيه على ترادفهما ههنا، ويمكن أن يكون نسخا اصطلاحيا عند من يقول: إن رفع الإباحة الأصلية، ورفع ما في الجاهلية، أو في الشرائع

نسخاً؛ لأن بيع الحر كان في شريعة يوسف عليلا، وبيع المضامين والملاقيح
أربع
والنهي عن بيع الحر إلخ لا يقال: إن هذا تكرار ؛ لأنه ذكر فيما تقدم أن بيع الحر قبيح لعينه، فلا يكون مشروعًا بأصله؛ لأنا نقول: ذكر هناك باعتبار أقسام القبح وههنا باعتبار ما ورد على القاعدة به سؤال كذا قيل. (القمر) مجاز إلخ للاتصال بين النفي والنهي صورة؛ لوجود حرف النفي فيهما، ومعنى، لأن الإعدام منظور فيهما وصلية وإن كان اقتضاء النهي العدم من قبل العبد بالاختيار واقتضاء النفي العدم من الأصل (القمر) حرمة القرابة كحرمة الأم وإن علت وحرمة البنت وإن سفلت. (القمر) أو حرمة المصاهرة وهي حرمات: حرمة أب الواطي، وابنه على الموطوءة، وحرمة أم الموطوءة وبنتها على الواطي. (القمر) بطريق المجاز من قبيل استعمال صيغة الإنشاء أعني النهي في الإخبار أعني النفي. (القمر) وهؤلاء: أي الحر، والمضامين والملاقيح ليسوا بمال، وقد مر حال مالية الحر فتذكر (القمر) وهن: أي المحارم محرمات ورده في الصبح الصادق : بأن نكاح المحارم نكاح حقيقة؛ لأن نكاحهن كان جائزا في الشرع السابق، وبالنسخ لا يبطل المحلية فالمحل قابل كيف؟ وأن النكاح ليس إلا لازدواج بين الرجل والمرأة لا غير. (القمر) ويمكن إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقرير الدخل: أن النهي لا يمكن أن يكون مجازا عن النسخ؛ لأن النسخ لابد له من وجود الناسخ والمنسوخ في شريعة واحدة و لم يجز بيع الحر في شريعة النبي الله أصلاً، وتقرير الرد ظاهر. (السنبلي)
Section on the Specific: Explanation of the types of foulness. Among them is benefit for one of the two contracting parties, or for the object of contract that is eligible to claim rights. And selling for wine and similar things: all of that is prescribed in terms of its essence, but corruption is due to an additional condition. So it becomes beneficial for ownership after the buyer takes possession, as does fasting on the day of Nahr being prescribed in terms of being a fast, and not prescribed in terms of the description which is turning away from hospitality. So the prohibition in all that attaches to the description, not to the essence. Then here there is an implied objection to Abu Hanifa, namely: that selling a free person, selling 'al-damin' and 'al-malaqih', and marriage to mahrams are among the prescribed acts, but here the prohibition does not fall on 'foul due to something else', rather on 'foul in itself' according to you, like the mother and the grandmother. And it contains benefit for one of the two parties... (continued) The author replied: The prohibition of selling a free person, al-damin, al-malaqih, and marrying mahrams is metaphorical for negation (nafy). So 'free person' is general for one free by origin or free by manumission. 'Al-damin' is plural of 'madmunah' meaning what is in the loins of fathers. 'Al-malaqih' is plural of 'maluqah' meaning what is in the wombs of mothers. 'Al-mahram' is general for prohibition by lineage or prohibition by marriage. In summary, the prohibition of these is interpreted as negation by way of metaphor. So it was abrogation due to absence of its subject... And it is possible that it is terminological abrogation according to those who say: lifting the original permissibility and lifting what was in Jahiliyyah or previous laws is called abrogation...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of prohibited acts (qabih) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It distinguishes between qabih li-aynihi (foul in itself) and qabih li-ghayrihi (foul due to something else). The author, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi or possibly the commentator (likely al-Qamar), explains that prohibitions in transactions such as selling wine or conditional sales attach to the description (e.g., the condition) not the essence. Therefore, the act remains legally valid in origin but becomes corrupt (fasid) due to the extraneous condition. The text then presents a potential objection against Imam Abu Hanifa: selling a free person, selling what is in loins and wombs, and marrying mahrams are considered prohibited in themselves by other schools, yet Abu Hanifa also considers them prohibited. How can he maintain that prohibitions on prescribed acts only fall on descriptions? The commentary answers by stating that these prohibitions are metaphorical (majaz) for outright negation (nafy). That is, such 'sales' or 'marriages' are null because they lack a valid subject (mahall): a free person is not property, and mahrams are not permissible spouses. Hence, the prohibition is not on a valid act but declares the act nonexistent. This is a core Hanafi position: many prohibitions that appear to be on acts are actually negations of the act's validity. The commentary also discusses the concept of abrogation (naskh) in this context, noting that the term is used metaphorically to mean lifting the original permissibility from previous laws.

Key Terms
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage; here the imperative of prohibition is used to convey negation (nafy) rather than a genuine command to refrain.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation; in this context, used metaphorically to mean lifting the original permissibility from previous laws.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the passage, why is the prohibition on selling a free person considered metaphorical for negation?
A) Because the free person is not property (mal), so there is no valid subject.
B) Because the sale of a free person was once permitted and later abrogated.
C) Because the imperative 'do not sell' is ambiguous.
D) Because the free person can consent to the sale.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats certain prohibitions as metaphorical negations when the act lacks a valid subject, preserving the principle that prohibitions on valid acts only attach to descriptions.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Prohibition of Non-Property Sales and Mahram Marriage
Explains the Hanafi interpretation of prohibitions as metaphorical negation, contrasted with Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that prohibition (nahy) can be metaphorical negation when the subject is absent, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument that all prohibitions indicate inherent ugliness.

Core Idea
Hanafi: nahy may be metaphorical negation; Shafi'i: nahy always implies inherent ugliness.

This passage delves into a fundamental usul dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the interpretation of prohibitions in specific cases. The Hanafis argue that the prohibition of selling free persons, mudamin, malaqih, and marrying mahram is not a real prohibition indicating inherent ugliness, but rather a metaphorical expression for negation due to the absence of the subject of the contract. Imam al-Shafi'i, conversely, holds that all prohibitions, whether in sensory or legal acts, indicate essential ugliness. Understanding this dispute illuminates the deeper methodological differences in how the schools approach textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the dispute is about the nature of prohibition (nahy) in specific cases: selling free persons, mudamin (what is in loins), malaqih (what is in wombs), and marrying mahram. Is the prohibition real (indicating ugliness) or figurative (negation due to absence of subject)?
The acts are prohibited by the Prophet ﷺ, but the schools differ on the legal significance of that prohibition.
2
Examine the Hanafi position and its evidence
The Hanafis argue that the prohibition is metaphorical (majaz) for negation. The subject (mahal) of sale is property, but free persons are not property; the subject of marriage is permissible women, but mahram are not permissible. Thus, the prohibition cancels the legal validity of the act, not its ethical status. They also note that some of these acts were permitted in earlier laws, so the Islamic prohibition abrogates that permissibility.
This reasoning applies the Hanafi usul principle that nahy can be figurative when the literal meaning is impossible due to missing conditions.
3
Examine the Shafi'i position and its evidence
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that prohibition always indicates essential ugliness (qabih li-aynihi), whether in sensory acts like zina or legal acts like fasting on 'Id. The subject matter does not affect the meaning of the prohibition. He analogizes prohibition to command: just as command indicates inherent goodness, prohibition indicates inherent ugliness.
This is based on the Shafi'i usul that nahy is absolute and carries the same force regardless of context.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument
The Hanafis respond that the prohibition in these cases cannot be real because the subject is absent. The analogy between command and prohibition holds only when the subject is valid. They distinguish between acts that are inherently ugly (like zina) and those that are invalid due to lack of condition (like selling a free person).
This response highlights the Hanafi emphasis on the reality of the subject (mahal) in determining the meaning of the prohibition.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Both schools agree that selling a free person or marrying a mahram is invalid (batil). However, for the Hanafis, the act is not inherently sinful but rather legally void due to missing conditions. For the Shafi'is, the act is inherently prohibited and thus sinful. This difference affects related rulings, such as whether the act can be considered a sin or just a null contract.
The practical effect is subtle but significant in terms of ethical classification and legal reasoning for analogous cases.

جمع صلب
فأجاب عنه المصنف وقال: والنهي عن بيع الحر، والمضامين والملاقيح، ونكاح المحارم مجاز عن النفي، فالحر عام من أن يكون حر الأصل، أو حر العتاقة. والمضامين جمع مضمونة، وهو ما في أصلاب الآباء والملاقيح جمع ملقوحة، وهو ما في أرحام الأمهات والمحارم عام من أن يكون حرمة القرابة أو حرمة المصاهرة وبالجملة فالنهي عن هؤلاء محمول على النفي بطريق المجاز ، فكان نسحًا لعدم محله أي فكان هذا النهي كله نسخا للمشروعية، أي إعداما وإبطالاً لعدم محل النهي؛ إذ محل البيع هو المال، وهؤلاء ليسوا بمال ومحل النكاح المحللات، وهنّ محرمات بالنص، وفي إيراد لفظ النسخ بعد النفي تنبيه على ترادفهما ههنا، ويمكن أن يكون نسخا اصطلاحيا عند من يقول: إن رفع الإباحة الأصلية، ورفع ما في الجاهلية، أو في الشرائع السابقة يسمى نسخاً؛ لأن بيع الحر كان في شريعة يوسف عليه السلام، وبيع المضامين والملاقيح كان في الجاهلية ونكاح بعض المحارم كان في الجاهلية وبعضها في الأديان السابقة. وقال الشافعي رضي الله عنه: النهي في البابين ينصرف إلى القسم الأول، شروع في بيان مذهب الشافعي رحمه الله، يعني أن عنده النهي في كل من الأفعال الحسية والأفعال الشرعية ينصرف إلى القبح لعينه فحرمة الزنا والخمر وحرمة صوم النحر عنده سواء. قولاً بكمال القبح حال بمعنى الفاعل أي حال كونه قائلاً بكمال القبح وهو القبح لعينه، أو مفعول له أي لأجل قوله بكمال القبح كما قلنا في الحسن في الأمر؛ لأن من مذهبنا أن الأمر المطلق الخالي عن القرينة يقع على الحسن لعينه قولاً بكمال الحسن، فلا يكون صوم يوم العيد سببًا للثواب عنده ولا البيع الفاسد موجبًا للملك بعد القبض، وإنما شبه الشافعي محله النهي بالأمر؛ لأن النهي في اقتضاء القبح حقيقة كالأمر في اقتضاء الحسن فينبغي أن يكونا على السواء. النهي والأمر وبعضها كنكاح الأخت كان في شريعة آدم عليه السلام في "التوضيح": نكاح الأخت من بطن واحد لم يكن جائزا في شريعة آدم عليه السلام، وكانت السنة الإلهية ولادة ذكر مع أنثى ببطن واحد والمشروع أن يتزوج كل ذكر أنثى من بطن آخر، وكان النكاح بين التوأمين حرامًا. سواء مع الزنا وشرب الخمر من الأفعال الحسية، وصوم يوم النحر من الأفعال الشرعية، فكل من هذه الأفعال ليس مشروعًا أصلاً عند الشافعي رضي الله عنه، لا وصفا ولا أصلاً بل يكون باطلاً. قولاً بكمال القبح فإن النهي مطلق فينصرف إلى القبح الكامل، وهو القبح لعينه، فإن القبح لغيره الوصفي قبح من وجه دون وجه، فلا يكون كاملاً. بمعنى الفاعل إلى دفع دخل تقريره: أن الحال يكون محمولاً على ذيه، ولا يجوز الحمل ههنا؛ لأن الحال وهو القول مصدر، وذو الحال وهو الشافعي ذات محضة وحمل المصدر على الذات لا يجوز، فأجاب: بأن المصدر ههنا بمعنى الفاعل، وحمل الفاعل على الذات صحيح. أي لأجل قوله إلخ: هذا دفع لما يتوهم من أن حذف اللام من المفعول له إنما يجوز إذا كان فاعله وفاعل الفعل المعلل به واحدا وههنا فاعل الفعل المعلل به هو الشافعي ولا يعرف من لفظ القول كون الشافعي فاعلاً له، فأجاب بأن قولاً في الأصل كان لقوله إلخ والضمير المجرور فيه راجع إلى الشافعي، وهو فاعل لفظ القول ثم حذف الضمير وجعل لفظ القول منصوبًا وتحقق شرط حذف لام المفعول له كما قلنا :إلخ تنظير لما تقدم، وقياس لقبح المنهي على حسن المأمور به. حقيقة ولهذا لا يصح نفيه بأن يقول : فهي الشارع لا تقتضي القبح.
Section on the Specific. Explanation of the divisions of ugliness. He replied and said: The prohibition of selling a free person, mudamin, malaqih, and marrying mahram is a metaphor for negation. 'Free' is general, whether free by origin or free by manumission. Mudamin is the plural of mudmana, which is what is in the loins of fathers; malaqih is the plural of mulaqiha, which is what is in the wombs of mothers. Mahram is general, whether by kinship or by marriage. In summary, the prohibition of these is interpreted as negation by way of metaphor. So it was abrogation due to lack of subject: meaning this whole prohibition was abrogation of legality, i.e., nullification and cancellation, because of the absence of the subject of the prohibition. For the subject of sale is property, and these are not property. The subject of marriage is permissible women, and these are forbidden by text. In using the word 'abrogation' after 'negation', there is a hint that they are synonymous here. It can be terminological abrogation according to those who say that removal of original permissibility or removal of what was in Jahiliyyah or previous laws is called abrogation. Because selling a free person was in the law of Yusuf (peace be upon him), selling mudamin and malaqih was in Jahiliyyah, and marrying some mahrams was in Jahiliyyah and some in previous religions. Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him) said: The prohibition in both categories refers to the first division. Beginning of the explanation of the madhhab of al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him), meaning that in his view, the prohibition in both sensory acts and legal acts refers to ugliness in itself, so the prohibition of zina and wine and the prohibition of fasting on the day of Nahr are the same with him. Saying with complete ugliness, as a state meaning the subject, i.e., being a speaker of complete ugliness which is ugliness in itself, or as an object of purpose, i.e., for his saying with complete ugliness, as we said regarding good in the command. Because it is part of our madhhab that the absolute command free from context applies to good in itself, saying with complete good, so fasting on the day of 'Id is not a cause for reward with him, nor does a corrupt sale cause ownership after possession. He only likened, may Allah be pleased with him, the place of prohibition to command, because prohibition in requiring ugliness is real like command in requiring good, so they should be equal. Prohibition and command. And some, like marrying the sister, was in the law of Adam (peace be upon him). In al-Tawdih: Marrying a sister from the same womb was not permissible in the law of Adam (peace be upon him); the divine practice was the birth of a male and female from one womb, and the law was that each male marries a female from another womb, and marriage between twins was forbidden. Both with zina and drinking wine from sensory acts, and fasting on the day of Nahr from legal acts, so all these acts are not lawful at all according to al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him), neither in description nor in origin, but are void. Saying with complete ugliness, because prohibition is absolute so it refers to complete ugliness, which is ugliness in itself; for ugliness due to something else is descriptive ugliness, ugly from one aspect not from another, so it is not complete. Meaning the subject, to rebut a doubt: The state must be carried on its subject, and it is not permissible to carry it here, because the state (speaking) is a verbal noun, and the subject (al-Shafi'i) is a concrete essence, and carrying a verbal noun on an essence is not permissible. He replied: The verbal noun here means the active participle, and carrying the active participle on the essence is correct. That is, for his saying etc.: This rebuts the misconception that dropping the 'lam' from the object of purpose is only allowed when its doer and the doer of the verb explained by it are the same, and here the doer of the verb explained is al-Shafi'i, and it is not known from the word 'saying' that al-Shafi'i is the doer. He replied that 'qawlan' originally was 'li-qawlihi' etc., and the linked pronoun refers to al-Shafi'i, who is the doer of the word 'saying', then the pronoun was dropped and the word 'saying' was made accusative, and the condition for dropping the 'lam' of the object of purpose was fulfilled. As we said etc.: a parallel for what came before, and an analogy for the ugliness of the prohibited on the goodness of the commanded. Real, and for this reason it is not correct to negate it by saying: the Shar'i does not require ugliness.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the interpretation of prohibitions in specific cases. The text begins by explaining the Hanafi position: the prohibition of selling free persons, mudamin (what is in the loins of fathers), malaqih (what is in the wombs of mothers), and marrying mahram relatives is not a real prohibition indicating inherent ugliness, but rather a metaphorical expression for negation. This is because the subject of sale is property, and these free persons or potential slaves are not property; similarly, the subject of marriage is permissible women, while mahram women are explicitly forbidden. Thus, the prohibition here effectively cancels the legal validity of such acts, which the Hanafis call 'abrogation' in a broad sense. The Hanafis further argue that these acts were once permitted in previous laws (e.g., selling free persons in the law of Yusuf, marrying some mahrams in Jahiliyyah), so the Islamic prohibition abrogates that permissibility. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i holds that prohibition always indicates essential ugliness (qabih li-aynihi), whether in sensory acts like zina or legal acts like fasting on the day of Nahr. For him, all such prohibitions are absolute and render the acts inherently corrupt. The passage then delves into linguistic and grammatical analysis of the terms used, defending the Hanafi interpretation against potential objections. It also discusses the analogy between command (amr) and prohibition (nahy), showing that just as command indicates inherent goodness, prohibition indicates inherent ugliness in the Shafi'i view. The text clarifies that for Hanafis, the prohibition in these specific cases is metaphorical and not real, thus the acts are not inherently ugly but rather invalid due to lack of proper subject. This is a key difference in usul methodology between the schools.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): The textual prohibition, which in Hanafi usul can be real (indicating inherent ugliness) or metaphorical (indicating negation or invalidity).
مجاز (majaz): Figurative usage, where the word is used for something other than its original meaning due to a connection (here prohibition used to mean negation).
قبح (qubh): Ugliness or impermissibility; divided into qabih li-aynihi (inherently ugly) and qabih li-ghayrihi (ugly due to something else).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view explained in this passage, why is the prohibition of selling a free person considered metaphorical (majaz) rather than real?
A) Because free persons are not property, so the subject of sale is absent.
B) Because the Prophet ﷺ used metaphor in all prohibitions.
C) Because selling a free person was permitted in early Islam.
D) Because the Hanafis deny the concept of inherent ugliness.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis interpret certain prohibitions as metaphorical negation when the subject is absent, a key methodological difference from the Shafi'i insistence on real prohibition indicating inherent ugliness.

Can a Prohibited Act Be a Legal Cause? Four Branches of Hanafi Rationale
Inter-madhhab dispute on prohibited acts as legal causes with four applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether a prohibited act can serve as a valid legal cause (sabab), and apply the Hanafi distinction to the four branches (tayammum, ghasb, sinful travel, kafir seizure).

Core Idea
A prohibited act can still be a legal cause if the prohibition is accidental, not inherent.

In this passage, the author presents a fundamental usul dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i. The issue is whether an act that is forbidden (haram) can still be a valid cause (sabab) for a legitimate legal ruling. The Hanafis argue that as long as the act itself is not inherently ugly (qabih li-dhatihi), it can still produce its normal legal effect. The Shafi'is maintain that any prohibited act, regardless of the nature of its prohibition, cannot be a cause for something lawful. The passage then branches into four specific applications: tayammum, usurpation (ghasb), sinful travel for concessions, and seizure by a disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Read the opening lines to find the fundamental legal question: Can a prohibited act (haram) serve as a valid cause (sabab) for a legitimate ruling (mashru')? Note that the author contrasts 'indana' (with us, Hanafi) and the Shafi'i position.
This is an usul al-fiqh dispute about the nature of causation, not a specific fiqh ruling.
2
State the opposing schools
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that any prohibited act cannot be a cause for something legitimate because it is repugnant to the Lawgiver's intention. The Hanafi school distinguishes: the act itself may be permissible in essence; the prohibition may be accidental (li-ghayrihi) and does not strip the act of its causal efficacy.
The Hanafi position is presented as the default ('indana'), indicating tarjih toward it.
3
Extract the Hanafi rationale
The key reasoning is that ugliness (qubh) can attach to the act accidently. For example, travel is not inherently ugly; the sin of rebellion is separate. Therefore, the act (travel) remains suitable as a cause for concessions. Similarly, in ghasb, the act of taking property is forbidden, but the consequence (ownership after compensation) follows from the need to avoid double compensation.
This is a rational (talil) argument based on distinguishing essence from accident.
4
Walk through the four branches
Examine each branch: (1) Tayammum: earth purifies as a substitute for water, not because it is inherently purifying. (2) Ghasb: usurper owns after guarantee to prevent combining two compensations. (3) Sinful travel: concessions apply to all travelers because the sin is separable. (4) Kafir seizure: disbeliever owns Muslim property taken into dar al-harb because protection is lost.
In branches 2-4, the Shafi'i objection is that the prohibited act cannot cause a lawful effect; Hanafi response uses the inherent/accidental distinction.
5
Synthesize the key distinction
The Hanafi school uses the concept of 'qabih li-dhatihi' (inherently ugly) vs. 'qabih li-ghayrihi' (accidentally ugly). Acts that are inherently ugly (e.g., lying, murder) cannot be causes. Acts that are accidentally ugly (e.g., travel for rebellion, seizure via usurpation) can still be causes because the ugliness is not in the act's essence.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi usul and has significant practical consequences in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص
بيان أقسام القبح
في إفادة الطهارة أن التراب إنما يُطهر الأحداث لأجل قيامه مقام الماء لا من حيث نفسه. ولا يفيد الغصب الملك، عطف على "لا تثبت"، وتفريع ثانٍ للشافعي له؛ وذلك لأن الغصب حرام ومعصية، فلا يكون سببًا لأمر مشروع هو الملك إذا هلك المغصوب، وقضى ملك الغاصب المغصوب عليه بالضمان، وعندنا يملك الغاصب المغصوب بعد الضمان فيملك أكسابه الباقية في يده، وينفذ بيعه الماضي؛ لأنه لو لم يملك الغاصب المغصوب بل بقي في ملك المالك لاجتمع البدلان في ملكه، وهو الأصل مع الضمان، وذلك لا يجوز، فلما ملك المالك الضمان يجب أن يملك الغاصب المغصوب، فالضمان عنده بمقابلة اليد الفائتة عن الملك، وعندنا بمقابلة الملك الفائت إلا في المدبر، فإنه إذا غصب رجل مدبر أحدٍ، وهلك في يده يضمنه ولا يملكه جبرًا ليده الفائتة.
ولا يكون سفر المعصية سببًا للرخصة تفريع ثالث للشافعي؛ وذلك لأن سفر المعصية وهو سفر الآبق، وقاطع الطريق، والباغي معصية وحرام، فلا يكون سببًا لمشروع، وهو الرخصة في إفطار الصوم، وقصر الصلاة، وعندنا تعم الرخصة للمطيع والعاصي جميعا؛ لأن السفر ليس قبيحا في نفسه بل القبيح هو المعصية مجاور له منفك عنه، فيصلح سببًا للرخصة.
ولا يملك الكافر مال المسلم بالاستيلاء تفريع رابع للشافعي له؛ وذلك لأن استيلاء الكافر على مال المسلم، وإحرازه بدار الحرب أمر حرام ومحظور، فلا يصلح أن يكون سببًا لملكه، وعندنا يكون ذلك سببًا لملكه؛ لأن الحفظ إنما يكون بالملك أو باليد، فإذا أخذوه وأدخلوه في دارهم فات منا اليد والملك، فكان استيلاؤهم
Section on the Specific: Explanation of the categories of ugliness. Concerning the effect of purification: That earth purifies minor impurities only because it stands in place of water, not from itself. And usurpation does not confer ownership — this is an appendix to 'does not establish', and it is a second branch for al-Shafi'i; that is because usurpation is prohibited and sinful, so it cannot be a cause for a legitimate matter, which is ownership when the usurped object perishes, and the judge ruled for the owner's ownership of the usurped object upon guarantee. And in our view, the usurper owns the usurped object after the guarantee, so he owns its remaining earnings in his hand, and his past sale is executed; because if the usurper did not own the usurped object but it remained in the owner's ownership, then two compensations would combine in his ownership — the original and the guarantee — and that is not permissible. Since the owner owns the guarantee, the usurper must own the usurped object. So with him (al-Shafi'i), the guarantee is in exchange for the lost hand over the property; with us, it is in exchange for the lost ownership, except in the case of a mudabbar, for if a man usurps someone's mudabbar and it perishes in his hand, he guarantees it but does not own it, as a compensation for his lost hand. And travel for sin is not a cause for the concession — a third branch for al-Shafi'i; that is because travel for sin, which is the travel of a runaway slave, a highway robber, and a rebel, is sinful and prohibited, so it cannot be a cause for what is legitimate, namely the concession of breaking fast and shortening prayer. And with us, the concession includes both the obedient and the disobedient alike, because travel is not ugly in itself, rather the ugliness is the sin adjacent to it and separable from it, so it is suitable as a cause for the concession. And a disbeliever does not own a Muslim's property by seizure — a fourth branch for al-Shafi'i; that is because a disbeliever's seizure of a Muslim's property and its acquisition in dar al-harb is a prohibited and forbidden matter, so it cannot be a cause for his ownership. And with us, that is a cause for his ownership, because protection is only through ownership or hand. When they take it and bring it into their land, our hand and ownership are lost, so their seizure becomes [a cause].
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about whether a prohibited act can serve as a legitimate cause (sabab) for a ruling. Imam al-Shafi'i held that a prohibited act cannot be a cause for something legitimate (mashru'), while the Hanafi school distinguishes between acts that are inherently prohibited (qabih li-dhatihi) and acts that are prohibited due to an external factor. The passage presents four branches (tafri'at): (1) Tayammum with earth: Shafi'i said earth purifies only because it substitutes water, not inherently; Hanafis agree but emphasize the substitution. (2) Usurpation (ghasb) and ownership: Shafi'i said usurpation is a sin so it cannot cause ownership; Hanafis said the usurper does own the stolen property after compensating the owner, because otherwise two compensations would combine. The guarantee compensates for lost ownership, not just lost hand. Exception: a mudabbar slave (promised freedom upon master's death) is not owned by the usurper even after guarantee because he is destined for freedom. (3) Travel for sin (e.g., a runaway slave, highway robber, rebel): Shafi'i said such travel is sinful so it cannot grant the concession of breaking fast and shortening prayer; Hanafis said travel itself is not sinful, the sin is separate (the rebellion), so the concession applies to all travelers. (4) A disbeliever seizing Muslim property in dar al-harb: Shafi'i said the seizure is illegal so it cannot cause ownership; Hanafis said once the property is taken into dar al-harb, the Muslim loses both hand and ownership, so the disbeliever owns it through seizure.

Key Terms
خاص (khaas): The specific; a term used in usul for a word with a specific meaning, but here the heading is 'Mabahith al-Khaas' referring to discussions about specific rulings
قبيح (qabih): Ugly/prohibited; includes both inherently prohibited (li-dhatihi) and accidentally prohibited (li-ghayrihi)
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking another's property without right
ضمان (daman): Guarantee/compensation; payment of value or replacement
رخصة (rukhsah): Concession; a relaxation of the original ruling due to necessity or hardship (e.g., breaking fast while traveling)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the usurpation is a valid contract of sale
B) Because otherwise two compensations would combine in the owner's possession
C) Because the usurper has physical control and that is sufficient for ownership
D) Because the owner has waived his ownership

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows a prohibited act to serve as a legal cause if the prohibition is accidental, not inherent, as exemplified in ghasb, sinful travel, and kafir seizure.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Definition of 'Aam (General) in Hanafi Usul
Defining 'aam (general) as a term of art in Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'amm and distinguish it from the definition that requires istighraq.

Core Idea
Al-'amm is a word covering multiple individuals with shared boundaries — no condition of exhaustive inclusion (istighraq) required in one definition.

In this passage, the author defines 'amm (general) as a key term in usul al-fiqh. This definition is foundational for understanding how general texts (e.g., 'kill the polytheists') are treated in Hanafi legal theory. The author presents a definition that does not require istighraq, in contrast to the definition of the muhaqqiqun (verifiers) who add that condition. By grasping this definition, you will build a proper understanding of how general terms operate in deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition structure
Break down the definition phrase: 'ما تناول أفرادًا متفقة الحدود على سبيل الشمول'. Identify each component: 'ma' (expression of generality), 'yatadawwalu' (encompasses), 'afradan' (individuals), 'muttafiqat al-hudud' (identical boundaries), 'ala sabili al-shumul' (comprehensively).
This structure shows that 'amm is a word, not a meaning.
2
Distinguish 'amm from khass
Note that the definition explicitly excludes khass (specific) forms such as proper nouns, dual, and explicit specifics. The author says 'by his statement encompasses individuals, he excludes the specific'.
The dual (muthanna) is considered a form of khass because it specifies two individuals.
3
Understand the two definitions
Recognize that there are two definitions: one without the condition of istighraq (exhaustive inclusion) as adopted by the author following Fakhr al-Islam, and one with istighraq as adopted by the muhaqqiqun. The second definition adds 'encompassing all that it can include'.
The second definition is more restrictive; the first is the one used by many Hanafi scholars.
4
Clarify 'identical boundaries'
The passage clarifies that 'identical boundaries' does not mean identical essences (mahiyyat). For example, 'animals' includes different species but all share the universal meaning 'living creature'. This refutes a potential objection.
This nuance is crucial for applying the definition to words like 'human' or 'polytheist'.
5
Apply to an example
Test a word like 'al-mushrikun' (the polytheists). It encompasses all individuals who share the boundary of shirk, even though they may differ in specific beliefs. According to the first definition, it is 'amm without requiring that it cover every polytheist everywhere; specification (takhsis) can later exclude some.
This demonstrates the practical utility of the definition for legal interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لما فرغ المصنف له عن بيان الخاص بأحكامه وأقسامه، شرع في بيان العام. [تعريف العام] فقال: وأما العام فما تناول أفرادًا متفقة الحدود على سبيل الشمول، فكلمة 'ما' عبارة العموم لا يجري في المعاني والعام من أقسام وجوه النظم وضعًا لأن لفظ موضوع؛ كالخاص، وبقوله : يتناول أفراداً خرج الخاص، أما خاص العين فظاهر، التعرض على محل غير معصوم وهو المال المستولى عليه ثم اعلم أن عصمة المال عبارة عن كون الشيء محرم محصنًا لحق الشرع أو لحق العبد. (القمر) لا يجري إلخ : ظاهره أن المعاني لا تتصف بالعموم لا حقيقة ولا مجازا على ما قيل، وقال أكثرهم : إن المعاني تتصف بالعموم مجازاً ، وقال بعضهم: باتصاف المعاني به حقيقة كما أن اللفظ يتصف بالعموم حقيقة، والتفصيل يطلب من المطولات. (القمر) من أقسام وجوه إلخ إضافة الأقسام إلى الوجوه بيانية، فإن الوجوه هي الأقسام على ما قد مرّ الخاص ومنه المثنى، فإنه يتناول فردين لا فردًا. (القمر)
Then, when the author finished explaining the particular (al-khass) with its rulings and divisions, he began explaining the general (al-amm). He said: As for al-amm, it is what encompasses individuals having identical boundaries in a comprehensive manner. The word 'ma' is the expression of generality, and it does not apply to meanings. Al-amm is one of the divisions of the aspects of structure based on usage, because it is a word, like al-khass. By his statement 'encompasses individuals', he excludes the particular. As for particular of the entity, it is obvious. Then know that the protection of property means that the thing is forbidden and fortified for the right of the Shari'ah or the right of the servant. Commentary
This passage is a definition of 'aam (general) from the Hanafi usul perspective. The author, following Fakhral-Islam, defines 'amm as a word that encompasses individuals (afrad) that share common boundaries (hudud) in a comprehensive manner (shumul). This definition does not require istighraq (stipulating inclusion of all individuals), which is a later additional condition held by other scholars. The text explains that 'amm is a type of 'word' (lafz), like khass, and it excludes the specific. The glosses clarify that 'individuals with identical boundaries' means individuals that agree in the application of the universal meaning, not that they have identical essences (e.g., 'animals' includes different species). The discussion also notes that meanings (ma'ani) are not literally general, though some scholars allow metaphorical generality. The definition is foundational for understanding how 'amm operates in Hanafi legal theory, particularly in deriving rulings from general texts. The text also touches on the difference between two definitions of 'amm: one without condition of istighraq (the author's) and one with it (the muhaqqiqun's).

Key Terms
العام (al-'amm): A word that encompasses multiple individuals having identical boundaries in a comprehensive manner.
أفراد متفقة الحدود (afrad muttafiqat al-hudud): Individuals that share the same definition or descriptive boundary (hadd) in the sense of the universal meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'amm is a word covering individuals with shared boundaries, and it does not require exhaustive inclusion (istighraq) as a built-in condition.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Definition of al-'Aam (General) in Hanafi Usul
Defining the general term and distinguishing it from specific terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of al-'Aam and distinguish it from al-Khaas, numerals, homonyms, and negated indefinites, identifying the internal Hanafi dispute over istighraq.

Core Idea
Al-'Aam encompasses all individuals of a kind collectively; its definition excludes specific terms, numerals, homonyms, and negated indefinites.

This lesson defines the foundational usul term al-'Aam (general) in the Hanafi madhhab. We will dissect the author's definition phrase by phrase, understand what each exclusion means, and note the internal Hanafi disagreement on whether comprehensiveness (istighraq) is required. This sets the stage for understanding how Hanafi usul treats general texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Read the opening sentence: al-'Aam is 'what encompasses individuals whose definitions are identical, in an inclusive manner'. Note that the author uses 'ma' as a general expression.
The definition focuses on individuals (afrad), not meanings.
2
Exclude specific terms (al-Khaas)
The phrase 'encompasses individuals' excludes al-Khaas (specific). Understand the three types of specific: particular entity, genus/species, and numerals/homonyms.
Genus/species terms cover a universal concept, not individuals. Numerals refer to parts; homonyms refer to multiple meanings.
3
Interpret 'identical definitions'
This phrase excludes homonyms because they encompass individuals with different definitions.
Some scholars treat this as a restriction; others as mere explanation.
4
Interpret 'inclusive manner'
This excludes the negated indefinite (nakira manfiyya), which refers to individuals one by one (badaliyya) rather than collectively.
E.g., 'ma ra'aytu rajulan' means 'I did not see any man' – alternative, not inclusive.
5
Understand the istighraq dispute
The author follows Fakhr al-Islam, who does not require comprehensiveness (istighraq). Thus both definite and indefinite plurals are 'aam. Sahib al-Tawdih requires istighraq, making indefinite plural an intermediate category.
This is an internal Hanafi khilaf with practical consequences for interpreting general texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث العام
تعريف العام

وأما العام فما تناول أفرادًا متفقة الحدود على سبيل الشمول، فكلمة "ما" عبارة العموم لا يجري في المعاني والعام من أقسام وجوه النظم وضعًا. وبتناول أفراداً خرج الخاص، أما خاص العين فظاهر، وأما خاص الجنس والنوع؛ فلأنه يتناول مفهومًا كليًا، أو فردًا واحدًا يحتمل الصدق على كثيرين وليس هو بموضوع للأفراد بنفسه، وكذا خرج أسماء العدد؛ لأنه يتناول الأجزاء نحو ثلاثة وأربعة وأمثالهما دون الأفراد، وكذا يخرج به المشترك؛ لأنه يتناول معاني لا أفرادًا. ثم قوله: "متفقة الحدود على سبيل الشمول" لبيان تحقيق ماهية العام لا للاحتراز، وقيل: "متفقة الحدود" احتراز عن المشترك؛ لأنه يتناول أفرادًا مختلفة الحدود، و"على سبيل الشمول" احتراز عن النكرة المنفية، فإنها تتناول الأفراد على سبيل البدلية دون الشمول، وإنما اكتفى المصنف له بالتناول دون الاستغراق اتباعًا لفخر الإسلام فإنه لا يشترط عنده في العام: الاستغراق لجميع الأفراد، فالجمع المعرف والمنكر كله عام، وعند صاحب "التوضيح" يشترط في العام الاستغراق، فيكون الجمع المنكر واسطة بين العام والخاص.
Section on al-'Aam (the General)
Definition of al-'Aam

As for al-'Aam (the general), it is that which encompasses individuals whose definitions are identical, in an inclusive manner. The word 'ma' (what) is an expression of universality; it does not run in meanings, and al-'Aam is among the categories of aspects of textual structures by convention. By [the phrase] 'it encompasses individuals', the specific (al-khaas) is excluded. As for the specific of a particular entity, it is obvious. As for the specific of genus and species, it is because it encompasses a conceptual meaning or a single individual that can possibly apply to many, but it is not posited for individuals by itself. Similarly, numerals are excluded because they encompass parts like three and four and their like, not individuals. Likewise, the homonym (al-mushtarak) is excluded by it because it encompasses meanings, not individuals. Then his statement: 'whose definitions are identical, in an inclusive manner' is to explain the reality of the general, not as a restrictive condition. And it is said: 'whose definitions are identical' is a restriction against the homonym, because it encompasses individuals of different definitions. And 'in an inclusive manner' is a restriction against the negated indefinite (al-nakira al-manfiyya), because it encompasses individuals in an alternative manner, not inclusively. The author sufficed with 'encompassing' instead of 'comprehensiveness' (istighraq) following Fakhr al-Islam, for according to him, comprehensiveness of all individuals is not a condition for the general. Thus, the definite plural and the indefinite plural are both general. According to the author of al-Tawdih, comprehensiveness is a condition for the general, so the indefinite plural becomes intermediate between the general and the specific.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a definition of al-'Aam (the general term) from a Hanafi usul text. The author defines 'aam as a word that encompasses all individuals of the same kind in a collective manner. The phrase 'ma' refers to the concept of generality but does not apply to meanings directly; rather, it applies to words. The definition excludes specific terms: particular entity, genus/species terms (which denote a concept or a single individual that could apply to many but is not set for individuals themselves), numerals (which refer to parts, not individuals), and homonyms (which refer to multiple meanings, not individuals of one meaning). The clause 'identical definitions' is explained by some as exclusion of homonyms, and 'inclusive manner' excludes negated indefinites (like 'ma ra'aytu rajulan' meaning 'I did not see any man'), which refer to individuals in an alternative (one-by-one) fashion rather than collectively. The author follows Fakhr al-Islam in not requiring 'istighraq' (comprehensiveness of all individuals), so both definite and indefinite plurals are considered general. However, the author of al-Tawdih requires istighraq, making the indefinite plural an intermediate category. This is an internal Hanafi difference regarding the definition of 'aam.

Key Terms
العام (al-'Aam): A word that encompasses all individuals of a kind whose definitions are identical, in an inclusive manner, according to the author's definition.
الخاص (al-Khaas): A word that does not encompass all individuals; includes specific entity, genus, species, numerals, homonyms, etc.
استغراق (istighraq): Comprehensiveness of all individuals without exception; required for 'aam according to some Hanafi scholars but not according to Fakhr al-Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is excluded from 'aam by the phrase 'identical definitions' (متفقة الحدود)?
A) Numerals
B) Negated indefinite
C) Homonym
D) Specific of genus

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'aam is broad enough to include indefinite plurals, but internal disagreement exists on whether comprehensiveness is required.
